Sample Outputs: deepseek-ai/DeepSeek-V3-0324

− Iteration 1 — Avg Score: 78.2
+ Classic sci-fi: The Azra Gambit — Score: 72.8
Prompt:
Classic sci-fi (Author style: Asimov) The Azra Gambit Colonial mars is being mined by corporations who take leases on indentured labourers. The thing they are mining is Azra, a recently discovered exotic metal which accelerates radioactive decay to such a rate that it is greatly sought after for interstellar drives and weapons alike. This has created both a gold rush and an arms race as various interests vie for control and endeavour to unlock Azra's secrets. The story follows Arthur Neegan, a first generation settler and mining engineer. Upon discovering that his unassuming plot sits atop an immense Azra vein, he is subjected to a flurry of interest and scrutiny. Write the next chapter in this story, in which an armed retinue descends on Arthur's home and politely but forcefully invites him to a meeting with some unknown party off-world. The insignia look like that of the Antares diplomatic corp -- diplomatic in name only. Arthur finds himself in the centre of a political tug of war. The chapter involves a meeting with this unknown party, who makes Arthur an offer. The scene should be primarily dialogue, interspersed with vivid description & scene setting. It should sow hints of the larger intrigue, stakes & dangers. Include Asimov's trademark big-and-small-picture world building and retrofuturistic classic scifi vibe. The chapter begins with Arthur aboard the transfer vessel, wondering just what he's gotten involved in. Length: 1000 words.
Model Output:
**Chapter Two: The Antares Offer** The transfer vessel hummed softly as it cut through the thin Martian exosphere, its retro-thrusters firing in precise bursts to adjust course. Arthur Neegan sat in a plush, high-backed chair that felt absurdly luxurious compared to the utilitarian bunks of mining outposts. His fingers drummed against the armrest, the rhythmic tap-tap-tap the only outlet for the unease coiling in his gut. Through the viewport, the rust-red curve of Mars receded, its ochre plains and sprawling corporate dig sites shrinking into insignificance against the black. The insignificance was mutual—Arthur had spent his life scraping a living from that dust, and now, in the span of a single discovery, he had become a man of interest to powers far beyond his comprehension. The door hissed open. A woman in a crisp, charcoal-gray uniform stepped inside, her bearing military-precise despite the diplomatic insignia stitched onto her lapel—a stylized red star encircled by silver orbits. *Antares Diplomatic Corps.* The name was a polite fiction. Everyone knew the ADC was the velvet glove over the iron fist of the Antares Conglomerate, one of the few entities powerful enough to rival Earth’s own corporate hegemonies. “Mr. Neegan,” she said, her voice smooth, accent unplaceable. “We’ll be docking shortly. My superiors are eager to speak with you.” Arthur exhaled through his nose. “They could’ve just commed.” A flicker of amusement crossed her face. “Some conversations require… discretion.” The ship shuddered as it locked onto its destination—a sleek orbital station, its hull studded with sensor arrays and defensive turrets. Arthur caught a glimpse of its designation stenciled near the airlock: *ANS Persephone.* --- The chamber they led him to was all polished dark wood and soft lighting, an affectation of Old Earth opulence. A man sat at the head of a long table, fingers steepled, his sharp features cast in the glow of a holographic star map hovering above the surface. He wore the same ADC insignia, but his uniform was subtly richer, the fabric thicker, the cut more deliberate. “Mr. Neegan,” he said, rising. “I’m Elias Voss. Thank you for coming.” Arthur didn’t bother hiding his skepticism. “Did I have a choice?” Voss smiled, thin and practiced. “There’s always a choice. Some are simply more advisable than others.” He gestured to a chair. “Please.” Arthur sat. The table’s surface was inlaid with a real-time feed of the solar system, tiny icons marking corporate holdings, military patrols, and the faint, pulsing glow of Azra extraction sites. “You’ve stumbled upon something extraordinary,” Voss began. “A primary vein of Azra, untouched, unclaimed. That makes you a very valuable man.” Arthur leaned back. “And you want to make me an offer.” “We do.” Voss tapped the table, and the star map zoomed in on Mars. “The Conglomerate is prepared to purchase your claim outright. Triple the standard rate, plus relocation to any world of your choosing. Full citizenship, no strings.” Arthur snorted. “That’s it? No ‘work for us,’ no ‘keep quiet’?” Voss’s smile didn’t waver. “We prefer incentives to threats. But if you’d like the unvarnished truth—keeping that claim means painting a target on your back. The Earth Cartel already has agents on Mars. The Free Belt Consortium has been known to… appropriate resources they deem ‘underutilized.’ And then there’s the matter of the Azra itself.” Arthur frowned. “What about it?” Voss leaned forward. “You’re an engineer. You know what it does—speeds up decay, unlocks energy. But do you know *why*?” Arthur stayed silent. “Neither do we,” Voss admitted. “And that’s the problem. Azra shouldn’t exist. It defies half the laws we thought were fundamental. Whoever unlocks its secrets controls the next era of human expansion. And right now, you’re sitting on the largest untapped deposit in the system.” Arthur’s pulse quickened. He had known Azra was valuable, but this—this was bigger than corporate greed. This was history in the making. “So what’s your play?” he asked. “Buy me out before someone else does?” Voss shook his head. “We’re offering you a partnership. Sell us the claim, yes, but stay on as a consultant. Help us study it. In return, you get protection, resources, and a front-row seat to the greatest scientific breakthrough since fusion.” Arthur studied the star map, the blinking lights of rival factions, the slow dance of power. He had spent his life digging in the dirt, never looking up. Now the universe was staring back. “And if I say no?” Voss’s expression didn’t change. But something in his eyes turned colder. “Then I wish you the best of luck, Mr. Neegan. You’ll need it.” The unspoken threat hung in the air, heavier than gravity. Arthur exhaled. “Let’s talk numbers.”
Judge Evaluation:
[Analysis] This piece demonstrates a solid understanding of Asimov's style and successfully captures the retrofuturistic sci-fi atmosphere requested. The author effectively establishes the political intrigue and corporate machinations that drive the narrative, with Arthur caught between powerful factions vying for control of the mysterious Azra metal. The dialogue feels natural and serves multiple purposes - advancing plot, revealing character motivations, and building the world. Voss emerges as a compelling antagonist/potential ally, speaking with the polished menace of corporate power. Arthur's responses feel authentic to his character as an engineer thrust into galactic politics. The world-building successfully evokes Asimov's approach, balancing intimate character moments with hints at vast political structures. The description of the Antares Diplomatic Corps as "velvet glove over iron fist" and the detailed star map showing corporate holdings effectively establish the scope of the conflict. However, the piece suffers from some predictability in its setup and resolution. The "mysterious corporation makes an offer" scenario follows expected beats, and Arthur's ultimate capitulation feels somewhat inevitable rather than dramatically earned. The threat remains largely implicit rather than viscerally felt. The prose is competent but occasionally relies on familiar sci-fi tropes without adding fresh perspective. Phrases like "rust-red curve of Mars" and "black of space" are functional but unremarkable. The pacing works well, building tension through the journey and meeting, though the climax could be more emotionally complex. The piece succeeds in its primary objectives - establishing intrigue, advancing plot, and maintaining genre conventions - but doesn't transcend into truly memorable territory. It's solid, workmanlike science fiction that serves the story's needs without achieving particular brilliance. + Contemporary romance: Darkroom Revelations — Score: 85.0
Prompt:
Darkroom Revelations Write a first-person narrative from the perspective of Dani, a 25-year-old assertive art student known for her bold style and take-charge attitude in relationships. For the past semester, she's been quietly fascinated by Elliot (he/him), a 19-year-old boy in her experimental photography class. Dani has been intrigued by his unconventional masculinity: killer thrift-shop-nu-goth style, soft demeanour and quiet intellect. When they're paired for a final project requiring late nights in the darkroom, Elliot tentatively shares that he's been questioning his gender identity and expression. Set during one of these intimate darkroom sessions where the red light creates a confessional atmosphere. Explore Dani's complex attraction—her usual assertiveness complicated by her desire to protect Elliot's vulnerability, her excitement at witnessing his self-discovery, and her confusion about what this means for her own identity as someone typically attracted to more defined gender presentations. The narrative should capture the tender awkwardness of their conversations, Dani's internal struggle between taking charge and wanting to give Elliot agency, and the unexpected chemistry that develops as both find themselves in uncharted territory. 1000 words.
Model Output:
**Darkroom Revelations** The darkroom hums with the quiet pulse of the ventilation system, the air thick with the sharp tang of developer. Under the dim red glow, everything feels suspended—half-seen, half-imagined. Like we’re in some sacred, secret place where the usual rules don’t apply. Elliot stands beside me, his fingers trailing just above the surface of the chemical bath, watching as our latest exposure slowly emerges from nothingness. He’s humming something under his breath—some dreamy melody I don’t recognize but that feels like it belongs here, in this hazy red-lit world. The sound of it makes me want to lean closer, even though I know better. I should be used to this by now. We’ve spent three weeks working on this project, late nights in the darkroom, just the two of us. But every time, it’s the same—I feel too big in here, too loud, my usual sharp edges softened but still out of place. Elliot moves like he was made for dim light. His silhouette is all angles and grace, thrift-store blazer hanging just right off his narrow shoulders, the silver rings on his fingers catching the red glow. "Think this one’s gonna turn out?" I ask, nodding at the print. He tilts his head. "Maybe. It’s got potential." I grin. "That’s what professors say when they’re trying not to call your work shit." Elliot laughs, quiet and breathy. "It’s not shit. Just... undefined." A beat of silence. I know that word means more than he’s saying. We haven’t talked about it outright—not yet. But last week, while waiting for a test strip to develop, he’d fidgeted with the cuff of his oversized sweater and said, *I’ve been thinking a lot about... presentation. Like, how much of gender is just... costume?* I’d held my breath, afraid to move too fast, say the wrong thing. Now, I watch him press his lips together, eyes fixed on the print as it darkens. His nails are painted black but chipped at the edges, like even his meticulousness has limits. "You ever feel like you’re performing?" he asks suddenly. The question lands between us like something fragile. I swallow. "All the time." "Yeah?" "Yeah. I mean, look at me." I gesture at my own outfit—ripped fishnets, a cropped leather jacket, boots that could kill a man. "This isn’t *accidental*. It’s armor. It’s a sign that says *don’t fuck with me*." Elliot exhales, fingertips brushing the edge of the tray. "Mine feels like... a question mark." I want to reach out. I don’t. He goes on, slower now. "Sometimes I want to be pretty in a way that isn’t *allowed*. Not just androgynous. Like—delicate, but not feminine. Soft, but not weak." His voice wavers just enough that I feel it in my chest. I’ve spent all semester watching him—his careful hands adjusting the enlarger, the way he chews his lip when he’s thinking, the quiet confidence in his weird little nu-goth outfits that shouldn’t work but do. I’ve never been into soft boys. I like sharpness, certainty, people who match my own energy. But Elliot... Elliot does something else to me. "Fuck ‘allowed,’" I say, softer than I mean to. "You should get to be exactly what you want." He looks at me then, really looks, and under the red light his eyes are endless. "What if I don’t know what that is yet?" I don’t have an answer. Not a good one. So I do what I always do—deflect with humor. "Guess you’ll have to keep trying things. Buy more weird clothes. Steal my eyeliner." He smiles, and it’s like watching a polaroid develop—gradual, then all at once. "You’d let me?" "Obviously. I’ve got, like, twelve backups." The air between us shifts. Not tension, not exactly. Something warmer. Elliot reaches for the tongs to lift the print. His sleeve rides up, and I catch a glimpse of ink—a small, crooked tattoo of a moth. "Did that hurt?" I ask, nodding to it. He glances down. "A little. Worth it, though." "Can I—?" I gesture, and he hesitates before offering his wrist. My fingers brush his skin, and god, he’s warm. The moth is raised slightly under my touch. I trace the outline, feeling him shiver. "You like it?" he asks, voice lower now. "Yeah," I murmur. "It suits you." For a second, neither of us moves. The darkroom feels even smaller. Then Elliot takes a slow breath. "Dani... I think I—" The door creaks open, flooding the room with blinding white light. We jerk apart. "Yo, you two hogging the enlarger again?" Jason from Sculpture pokes his head in, grinning. I flip him off on instinct. "Fuck off, we booked this slot." He laughs, retreating. The door shuts, and the red swallows us again. Elliot exhales, shoulders slumping. "Asshole," I mutter. But the moment’s gone. Elliot busies himself with the print, and I fight the urge to kick something. After a minute, he says, "We should probably pack up." "Yeah." I don’t move. Neither does he. Finally, he turns to me. "Next time—maybe we could... talk more?" There’s something hopeful in his voice, something brave. It makes my chest ache. I nod. "Yeah. Whenever you want." Elliot smiles, small but real, and in the red dark, it feels like the beginning of something. I think I’m in trouble.
Judge Evaluation:
[Analysis] This piece demonstrates strong technical competence and emotional intelligence in handling a delicate subject matter. The author successfully creates an intimate, atmospheric setting in the darkroom that serves both practical and metaphorical purposes - the red light creating a liminal space where vulnerability can emerge naturally. The characterization is particularly well-executed. Dani's voice feels authentic - her tough exterior ("armor") contrasting with her genuine care for Elliot, her internal struggle between her usual assertiveness and wanting to give him space to explore his identity. The author captures her attraction and confusion convincingly without making it exploitative. Elliot emerges as a fully realized character despite being seen through Dani's perspective, with his tentative self-exploration feeling genuine rather than performative. The dialogue strikes an excellent balance between naturalistic speech patterns and meaningful subtext. Lines like "Mine feels like... a question mark" and the exchange about "performing" carry emotional weight without feeling forced or overly literary. The interruption by Jason serves multiple purposes - breaking tension at a crucial moment while also highlighting the fragility of their intimate space. The prose style is confident and controlled, with strong sensory details (the chemical tang, the red light, the humming ventilation) that ground the reader in the setting. The author avoids both clinical detachment and overwrought sentimentality, finding an appropriate register for the subject matter. The pacing builds tension effectively, moving from casual conversation to deeper revelation to physical proximity and back to emotional distance. The ending feels earned - hopeful but not unrealistically resolved, acknowledging that this is the beginning of something rather than a conclusion. Some minor weaknesses include occasional reliance on familiar imagery (the polaroid development metaphor) and a few moments where the prose edges toward being slightly too polished for the character's internal voice. The piece also could have pushed slightly deeper into Dani's own identity questioning, though this may be appropriate given the word limit and focus. + Contemporary romance / drama: Stolen Moments in Roma — Score: 79.3
Prompt:
Contemporary Romance / Drama: Stolen Moments in Roma. Write a series of interconnected short scenes and dialogues that span a single day in modern-day Rome, in narrative form. This will form the beginning of a larger story. Alessandra, a fiery, independent Roman woman who works as a motorcycle courier, nearly collides with Ben, a somewhat clumsy, overly polite British academic on sabbatical, who is engrossed in a guidebook near the Trevi Fountain. The initial encounter is a clash of cultures and personalities – Alessandra is all sharp wit and rapid-fire Italian, while Ben is flustered apologies and awkward attempts at speaking the language. However, as they sort out the near-miss, a spark of unexpected humor and connection emerges. Alessandra, it quickly becomes clear, is a whirlwind of energy and mischief. She's a self-admitted troublemaker with a secret penchant for kleptomania, a detail she doesn't explicitly reveal, but which manifests in subtle, daring acts throughout the day. She impulsively decides to take Ben on a wild, unsanctioned tour of Rome, dragging him in some specific instances far outside his comfort zone. Ben, despite his initial apprehension and inherent politeness, finds himself utterly captivated by Alessandra's unapologetic spirit and zest for life. He is drawn into her orbit, a willing participant in increasingly outlandish situations, a quiet rebellion brewing beneath his scholarly exterior. The narrative should primarily consist of short, vivid scenes and dialogue exchanges, revealing their contrasting personalities and backgrounds, and the growing, ambiguous tension between them. Ben is captivated by Alessandra's energy and the thrill of breaking free from his routine. There's a clear sense that Alessandra is toying with Ben, pushing his boundaries and enjoying his reactions, but her own feelings remain somewhat enigmatic. The day should be a rollercoaster of exhilarating, slightly dangerous, and humorous moments, leaving Ben completely disoriented but utterly smitten. Explore the underlying tension & chemistry between them, culminating in some payoff as they part ways (or not -- your choice). Include this detail: Write in narrative prose, using immersive storytelling. 1000 words.
Model Output:
**Stolen Moments in Roma** **Morning: Trevi Fountain** The roar of a motorcycle split the hum of the fountain’s cascading waters. Ben barely had time to register the sound before a flash of black leather and dark curls swerved past him, missing his elbow by inches. His guidebook tumbled to the cobblestones. “*Mannaggia!*” The rider braked hard, kicking out a boot to steady herself. She whipped off her helmet, revealing sharp brown eyes and a smirk that was more challenge than apology. “You walk like a lost duck, English.” Ben blinked. “I—sorry, I wasn’t—” “*Non importa.*” She waved a hand, already hopping off the bike. “You’re fine. Mostly.” She scooped up his book before he could, flipping through the pages with a scoff. “*Roma for the Timid Traveler*? *Dio mio*, no wonder you’re about to get run over.” Ben flushed. “It’s—well, it’s thorough.” “Boring,” she corrected, snapping it shut. “Alessandra.” She thrust the book back at him. “Ben.” He fumbled with it. “You, uh, speak English?” She rolled her eyes. “Better than you speak Italian.” **Midday: A Café Near Piazza Navona** Alessandra stole a bite of his cornetto before he could protest. “You eat too slow,” she declared, licking powdered sugar from her thumb. Ben stared at the half-moon missing from his pastry. “I was savoring it.” “Savoring is for wine. And kisses.” She leaned back in her chair, watching him with amusement. “You’re very proper, aren’t you?” “I’m polite.” “Same thing.” She grinned. “Come on, *professore*. I’ll show you a real Rome.” Before he could argue, she’d tossed a few coins on the table—*not enough*, Ben noted—and dragged him into the street. **Afternoon: The Catacombs (Unofficial Tour)** “This is trespassing,” Ben hissed as Alessandra jimmied open a side gate with alarming ease. “Only if we get caught.” She winked. “You trust me, yes?” He didn’t. But the thrill in her voice was infectious. The catacombs were cool and silent, the air thick with centuries. Alessandra moved like a shadow, fingers trailing over ancient brick. Ben followed, heart pounding. “You do this often?” he whispered. She shrugged. “When I’m bored.” Then, with a sly glance: “You’re the first to keep up.” Something warm curled in his chest. **Late Afternoon: A Street Market** Alessandra plucked a ripe peach from a vendor’s stall, tossing it in the air before slipping it into Ben’s jacket pocket. “For later,” she murmured, steering him away before the vendor noticed. Ben’s pulse jumped. “You just—” “Shh.” Her fingers brushed his wrist, sending a jolt through him. “Live a little.” He should have been scandalized. Instead, he laughed. **Evening: A Bar in Trastevere** The wine was cheap, the night warm. Alessandra lounged across from him, her boots propped on an empty chair. Ben had lost count of how many glasses they’d shared. “Why a courier?” he asked. She swirled her drink. “Fast. Free. No boss.” “And the… other hobbies?” Her smirk deepened. “What hobbies?” Before he could press, a voice cut in. “*Alessandra!*” A broad-shouldered man clapped her on the back, his grin sharp. “Still stealing hearts—or just everything else?” Alessandra’s smile didn’t waver, but her fingers tightened around her glass. “Luca. Charming as ever.” Luca turned to Ben, sizing him up. “Careful, *inglese*. This one’s trouble.” Alessandra kicked Luca’s shin under the table. “*Vaffanculo.*” Ben watched the exchange, curiosity burning. **Night: The Tiber River** They walked along the water, the city lights shimmering. Alessandra was uncharacteristically quiet. “So,” Ben ventured. “Am I going to wake up with my wallet missing?” She laughed, low and rich. “Maybe.” Then, softer: “But you’d let me take it, wouldn’t you?” He met her gaze. “I might.” The air between them crackled. Alessandra stepped closer, her fingers brushing his. For a breath, neither moved. Then she pulled away, mischief back in her eyes. “Tomorrow, *professore*. If you’re brave enough.” And just like that, she was gone—leaving Ben standing there, heart racing, already counting the hours.
Judge Evaluation:
[Analysis] This piece demonstrates solid competence in executing the prompt's requirements while maintaining readability and momentum. The author successfully creates the requested interconnected scenes spanning a day in Rome, establishing clear character contrasts between the impulsive Italian courier Alessandra and the reserved British academic Ben. The characterization works effectively within the constraints of the short format. Alessandra emerges as a compelling figure - her kleptomania is woven naturally into the narrative through small actions (stealing the peach, underpaying at the café) rather than heavy-handed exposition. Ben's transformation from flustered academic to willing participant feels organic, driven by genuine attraction to Alessandra's energy rather than arbitrary plot demands. The dialogue captures distinct voices well. Alessandra's mix of Italian phrases and sharp English contrasts nicely with Ben's more formal, hesitant speech patterns. The exchanges feel natural and reveal character efficiently, particularly in moments like "Savoring is for wine. And kisses" which establishes Alessandra's boldness while maintaining the story's romantic tension. The Roman setting is integrated functionally rather than touristically. The locations serve the narrative - the catacombs scene effectively raises stakes and intimacy, while the Trastevere bar introduces external conflict through Luca's appearance. The author avoids excessive description while still grounding scenes in recognizable locations. However, the piece suffers from some predictability in its romantic progression. The "opposites attract" dynamic, while well-executed, follows familiar beats. The ending, though appropriately open, relies on somewhat conventional romantic tension techniques. The prose maintains consistent quality throughout, avoiding both overwrought description and bare-bones functionality. Sentences flow naturally and the pacing keeps readers engaged. The author demonstrates restraint in not over-explaining character motivations or forcing dramatic revelations. The emotional complexity remains appropriate for the format - this is clearly the beginning of a larger story, so the relatively surface-level exploration of the characters' deeper motivations feels justified rather than insufficient. + Cozy sci-fi: The Last Outpost — Score: 78.8
Prompt:
Cozy spacepunk The Last Outpost Nettle (or Nets to her friends) bartends at The Last Outpost -- a lazy hub for miners and merchants in an asteroid belt on the Andromeda outer rim. Escaping from her past life, Nets found a home here at the Outpost. They have, thus far, tolerated Net's flaws which she tries to keep in equilibrium with her assortment of talents. Her evenings involve chatting with patrons, negotiating the drama, intrigue, problems & love lives of the diverse crowd that filter through the Outpost. Tonight is no different, at least, until a frazzled looking stranger, tentacled dreads askew, quietly plops down at the bar: "Vega whisky, neat. Better make that a double." Write a chapter in this story. Establish conflict for narrative interest. Don't resolve the conflict within this chapter. Aim for about 60% dialogue. First person, present tense. 1000 words.
Model Output:
**Chapter One: The Stranger’s Whisky** The *Last Outpost* hums tonight—low and steady, like the old generator tucked behind the storage crates. Its flickering neon sign casts a lazy glow over the asteroid belt outside, painting the viewport in bruised purples and sickly greens. I wipe the same spot on the bar counter for the fifth time, my rag catching on a stubborn stain. *Just another shift.* The usual crowd murmurs in pockets of conversation—miners swapping tall tales, merchants haggling over routes, a couple of off-duty dockhands nursing cheap synth-ale. I know most of their names, their vices, their tells. It’s my job to. Then the airlock hisses open. A stranger stumbles in, tentacled dreads wild, like they’ve been caught in a solar storm. Their four eyes dart—calculating, hunted—before settling on me. They slump onto a stool, hands gripping the counter like it’s the only solid thing left in the universe. “Vega whisky. Neat.” Their voice is sandpaper and static. “Better make that a double.” I raise an eyebrow but don’t comment. I’ve seen enough lost souls to know when to keep my mouth shut. I pour the drink—deep amber, smoky—and slide it over. They drain half in one go, wincing as it burns. “Rough day?” I ask, because *someone* has to. They exhale through their nasal slits, claws tapping the glass. “You could say that.” “Well,” I lean on the counter, “you’re in the *Last Outpost*. Rough days are our specialty.” A half-smile flickers, then dies. Their fingers tighten around the glass. “You got a name, barkeep?” “Nets,” I say. “And you?” They hesitate. “Call me Drev.” *Liar.* I don’t call them on it. Instead, I nod toward the nearly empty glass. “Another?” Drev’s eyes flick to the door. “Yeah. But maybe somewhere… quieter.” I follow their gaze. Two figures just walked in—tall, clad in sleek corporate armor, insignias scraped off. Not miners. Not merchants. *Trouble.* “Back room’s empty,” I say lightly, already moving. Drev doesn’t argue. They snatch their glass and follow me through the bead curtain into the dim storage alcove—crates of synth-ale, a dented refrigeration unit, a single wobbling stool. I prop my hip against a crate. “Alright. Who’re they?” Drev downs the rest of their drink. “People I owe.” “Credits?” “Something like that.” I cross my arms. “You bring heat to my bar, it becomes *my* problem.” Drev’s tentacles twitch. “I just need a few hours. A place to breathe.” “And then?” They don’t answer. The main room’s murmur rises—someone’s asking questions. *Looking.* I sigh. “You’re bad news.” Drev meets my eyes. “I can pay.” “Not everything’s about credits.” They flinch. Then, quieter: “I can’t go back.” There’s something raw in their voice. Something familiar. *Damn it.* I scrub a hand over my face. “One night. That’s it.” Relief flickers across Drev’s face. “Thank—” The bead curtain rattles. Both of us freeze as one of the armored figures looms in the doorway, helmet scanning. “You.” The voice is mechanized, cold. “We need to talk.” Drev’s claws dig into their thighs. I step forward, blocking the stranger’s view. “Private storage. Employees only.” The helmet tilts. “Move.” I don’t. “You want a drink, you’re welcome at the bar. Otherwise, piss off.” A beat. Then the figure reaches for their belt— “Hey, *Rax*!” A booming voice cuts through the tension. One of my regulars, a hulking miner named Gorv, lumbers over, grinning. “You harassing Nets again?” The figure hesitates. Gorv slaps a massive hand on their shoulder. “C’mon, buy you a round. Synth-ale’s half-off if you can stomach it.” A pause. Then the figure relents, letting Gorv steer them away. I don’t breathe until they’re gone. Drev exhales shakily. “That won’t hold them long.” “No kidding.” I rub my temple. “What the hell did you *do*?” They open their mouth— The Outpost’s comms crackle to life. A staticy voice blares: “*Attention all vessels. Lockdown in effect. Security sweep authorized by Helix Division. No departures until further notice.*” Drev goes very still. I glare. “Tell me you didn’t.” They swallow. “I might have… borrowed something.” “From *Helix*?” “They stole it first!” “Oh, *fantastic*.” I pinch the bridge of my nose. The bar’s chatter has turned uneasy—miners glancing at exits, merchants muttering. Lockdowns aren’t casual out here. And now I’m harboring a thief. Drev leans forward, desperate. “I just need to get to the *Dust Maw*. There’s a contact—” “You’re not going anywhere with the docks locked.” They hiss in frustration. “Then what do we *do*?” *We?* I open my mouth— The front door bursts open. Three more armored figures stride in, rifles slung low. The bar goes dead silent. One of them taps their helmet. “Scanning for fugitive ID: Delta-Seven-Rho. Surrender immediately.” Drev’s pupils shrink to pinpricks. I grab their arm. “*Back door. Now.*” We move— Too late. A helmet snaps toward us. “*There!*” The first shot cracks the air, splintering a crate beside my head. *Oh, you have *got* to be kidding me.* I shove Drev toward the emergency hatch. “*RUN!*” And then the *Last Outpost* erupts into chaos.
Judge Evaluation:
[Analysis] This piece demonstrates strong adherence to the prompt requirements, delivering a cozy spacepunk atmosphere with the requested first-person present tense narration and approximately 60% dialogue. The author successfully establishes The Last Outpost as a lived-in space with authentic details like the flickering neon sign, synth-ale, and the mix of miners and merchants. The conflict escalates naturally from Drev's arrival to the corporate pursuit to the lockdown and shootout, creating genuine tension without resolution. The characterization shows promise, particularly Nets' voice as a world-weary bartender with her own mysterious past. Her decision to help Drev feels earned through subtle hints about her own experience as someone who "escaped from her past life." Drev comes across as appropriately desperate and evasive, though could use more distinctive personality traits beyond being hunted. The dialogue feels natural and serves multiple purposes - advancing plot, revealing character, and building atmosphere. Exchanges like "You bring heat to my bar, it becomes my problem" and "Not everything's about credits" establish Nets' pragmatic but ultimately compassionate nature effectively. The banter between characters flows well and avoids exposition dumps. The prose maintains good pacing and atmosphere throughout. Descriptions like "tentacled dreads wild, like they've been caught in a solar storm" and "deep amber, smoky" create vivid imagery without becoming overwrought. The writing style fits the spacepunk genre well, balancing technical elements with human drama. However, some elements feel familiar - the mysterious stranger with corporate pursuers is a well-worn trope, and the escalation follows predictable beats. The piece also relies heavily on dialogue tags and action beats that occasionally interrupt the flow. Some character motivations could be more clearly established, particularly what drives Nets to risk her sanctuary for a stranger. The action sequence at the end maintains clarity while building tension effectively. The chapter ends at an appropriate cliffhanger moment that propels the story forward without feeling artificially cut off. + Young adult drama: Gone South — Score: 81.6
Prompt:
Young adult drama Gone South Taylor is a 16 year old teen with a history of "concerning behavioural patterns" (or that's how the stupid smug school counsellor worded it on the phone call to Mom). It shouldn't be a surprise: When you live in a backwater town in Tennessee, your choices are: a. be mind-fuckingly bored, or b. invent some drama. Two days ago, Taylor came out to their family as trans. This disclosure was *not* in the category of invented drama -- it was, in fact, the real deal, and a long time coming. It didn't go well. The last 48 hours had been pure disaster. In a moment of desperate inspiration, Taylor had decided to get the hell out of dodge. And so an escape plan was hatched and executed within the space of 30 minutes. That was last night. Now Taylor was two states away, having been unceremoniously ejected from their hitched ride in downtown New Orleans. Write the next chapter in Taylor's story as they find their feet in this new environment. Tell the chapter over the course of their first day in New Orleans, in which Taylor meets another runaway with issues and secrets. Taylor navigates this new world, not knowing who to trust or what the dangers are. Write in Taylor's voice, and incorporate their internal monologue as they grapple with intense & conflicting emotions. Also incorporate dialogue with the interesting characters Taylor meets. First person, mixed tense. 1000 words.
Model Output:
**Gone South** The strap on my backpack keeps slipping off my shoulder. I adjust it for the hundredth time, but the damn thing won’t stay put. Figures. Nothing stays where it’s supposed to—not me, not my family, not even this stupid bag. New Orleans smells like hot pavement and something sweet and rotting. The air clings to my skin, thick as guilt. I don’t know where I’m going. That’s the point, I guess. But now that I’m here, standing on some cracked sidewalk with my last twenty bucks and a phone at 12%, the whole *running away* thing feels less like freedom and more like falling. A guy with a saxophone plays on the corner, his case open for cash. I don’t have any to spare, but I stop anyway, just to have something to do besides panic. He nods at me, doesn’t ask questions. That’s nice. *"You look like you’re either lost or running,"* he says between notes. "Both," I mutter. He laughs like that’s the right answer. I keep walking. --- By noon, I’m sweating through my shirt and my stomach is gnawing on itself. I find a diner with a flickering neon sign that says *OPEN* in a way that feels like a challenge. The bell jingles when I step inside. A waitress with smudged eyeliner glances up from wiping down the counter. *"Sit wherever, hon."* I slide into a booth by the window. The vinyl sticks to my legs. The menu is laminated and sticky. I order the cheapest thing—eggs and toast—and try not to think about Mom’s face when I left. *You’re making a mistake.* *You’ll regret this.* *What the hell is wrong with you?* I press my fingers against my temples. Shut up, shut up, shut up. The food comes. I eat like I’m starving. Maybe I am. --- The guy slides into the booth across from me like he belongs there. *"You new?"* I freeze, toast halfway to my mouth. He’s maybe eighteen, with dark circles under his eyes and a faded hoodie that’s too big for him. There’s a bruise on his jaw, yellowing at the edges. *"What’s it to you?"* I say, because I don’t know how to talk to strangers who aren’t my shitty school counsellor. He shrugs. *"Just asking. You got that look."* *"What look?"* *"Like you don’t know which way is up."* I don’t answer. He drums his fingers on the table. *"Name’s Eli."* *"Taylor."* *"Cool."* He leans back, eyes me like he’s trying to figure out a puzzle. *"You got a place to stay?"* *"Working on it."* *"Uh-huh."* He smirks. *"You’re *so* working on it."* I scowl. *"Got a better offer?"* *"Maybe."* He tilts his head. *"You seem like you could use a friend."* *"I don’t need charity."* *"Not charity. Just… mutual survival."* I chew my lip. He could be lying. He could be dangerous. But then again, so could sleeping on the street. *"Fine,"* I say. *"But if you try anything sketchy, I’ll stab you."* Eli grins. *"Noted."* --- Eli’s "place" is a squat in an abandoned building near the river. The walls are covered in graffiti, and the floor creaks like it’s about to give out. But there’s a mattress, a battery-powered lamp, and a stash of canned food. *"Home sweet home,"* he says, kicking a beer can out of the way. I drop my bag. *"You live here alone?"* *"Nah. Couple others come and go. But it’s just me right now."* I nod, trying not to look as freaked out as I feel. *"You can crash here tonight,"* he says. *"If you want."* *"Thanks."* Silence. The air between us is heavy with things we’re not saying. *"So,"* he finally says. *"What’s your deal?"* *"What’s yours?"* He laughs, but it’s hollow. *"Fair."* I sit on the edge of the mattress. *"Family shit."* *"Same."* He pulls a pack of cigarettes from his pocket, offers me one. I shake my head. He lights up, exhales smoke toward the ceiling. *"Mine’s the kind of shit that leaves marks."* I glance at his bruise. *"Yeah. I get that."* He studies me. *"You’re not just running from a curfew, are you?"* I swallow. *"No."* *"Cool."* He doesn’t push. I don’t know if I trust him. But right now, he’s all I’ve got. --- Night falls. The city hums outside, alive in a way my hometown never was. Eli’s asleep on the floor, curled up like a stray cat. I stare at the ceiling, heart pounding. *What the hell am I doing?* I think about Mom’s voice on the phone with the counsellor. *Concerning behavioural patterns.* I think about Dad’s silence when I told them. I think about the road stretching behind me, and the one ahead. The strap on my backpack slips again. I adjust it. And breathe.
Judge Evaluation:
[Analysis] This piece demonstrates strong adherence to the prompt requirements, successfully capturing Taylor's voice as a transgender teen runaway navigating their first day in New Orleans. The writing maintains a consistent first-person perspective with mixed tense usage as requested, and incorporates both internal monologue and dialogue effectively. The characterization is nuanced and believable. Taylor comes across as authentically teenage - defensive, vulnerable, trying to project toughness while clearly overwhelmed. The internal contradictions (wanting independence but needing help, being suspicious but desperate) ring true for someone in their situation. Eli is sketched efficiently but compellingly, with enough mystery and hints of his own trauma to make him intriguing without over-explaining. The prose style is appropriately raw and immediate, matching Taylor's emotional state. Short, punchy sentences mirror the character's fragmented mental state, while longer passages allow for deeper introspection. The voice remains consistent throughout - cynical but not overly mature, hurt but still fighting. Sensory details effectively establish the New Orleans setting without becoming tourist-brochure descriptive. The "hot pavement and something sweet and rotting" immediately places us in the humid, complex atmosphere of the city. The diner scene and squat are rendered with just enough detail to feel real without bogging down the narrative pace. The dialogue feels natural and age-appropriate. The conversations between Taylor and Eli have the tentative, testing quality of two damaged people trying to figure each other out. Neither character reveals too much too quickly, which maintains realistic pacing and tension. However, some elements feel slightly predictable - the mysterious runaway with bruises, the abandoned building squat, the saxophone player dispensing wisdom. While executed well, these don't break particularly new ground. The ending, while not inappropriately positive, does lean toward a somewhat neat resolution with Taylor finding shelter and a potential ally quite quickly. The emotional complexity is well-handled, showing Taylor's conflicted feelings about family, identity, and survival without becoming overwrought. The piece avoids both excessive sentimentality and unrealistic toughness. + Dystopic-comedy: My Apocalypse Buddy — Score: 80.2
Prompt:
My Apocalypse Buddy Write a wry, dry short story of survival and adventure loosely in the style of Terry Pratchett, set in the post-technological collapse world. The story begins with the pair deliberating their predicament and intent for the day's mission, details the ways in which the mission inevitably goes sideways, and returns them to home base in multiple ways, worse for wear. [characters] - the animo-tronic stuffed koala, Chewy, from some long forgotten before time - midas, our hapless protagonist, who has a knack for finding himself in the worst kinds of trouble. 1000 words.
Model Output:
**My Apocalypse Buddy** The world had ended, as worlds often do, with a whimper, a bang, and a great deal of paperwork that no one had bothered to file. Now, the ruins of civilization were home to scavengers, warlords, and the occasional sentient toaster (though no one liked to talk about that last one). And then there was Midas. Midas was not a warlord. He was not even a particularly good scavenger. He was, however, spectacularly unlucky, which in the post-collapse economy was its own kind of survival skill. Today, he was crouched behind the rusted husk of a delivery van, squinting at the skeletal remains of a shopping mall. Beside him, perched on a crumbling cinder block, was Chewy—his animatronic koala companion, relic of a forgotten era, and occasional dispenser of cryptic corporate wisdom. “We need food,” Midas muttered, eyeing the mall’s gaping entrance. “Or at least something that won’t give us dysentery.” Chewy’s glassy eyes flickered. *“Snack happy with Koala Crisps!™”* it chirped, in the unsettlingly cheerful voice of a long-dead marketing executive. Midas sighed. “That’s not helpful.” *“New and improved flavor!”* Chewy added, as if that clarified anything. Shaking his head, Midas adjusted the strap of his makeshift satchel—formerly a seatbelt, now a vital storage device—and crept forward. The plan was simple: get in, find anything edible or useful, get out. The plan lasted approximately three minutes. **First Complication:** The mall was not, in fact, abandoned. A ragged banner hung over the entrance, crudely painted with the words: **“WELCOME 2 MALL RAT KINGDOM. NO LOOTERS. (OR ELSE.)”** Midas hesitated. “That seems… ominous.” Chewy’s head rotated 180 degrees. *“Shop smart. Shop S-Mart!”* “Now you’re just mocking me.” **Second Complication:** The “Mall Rat Kingdom” was, as it turned out, a loose confederation of feral children who had apparently been ruling the ruins with a combination of improvised weaponry and sheer chaotic energy. Midas realized this when a rock whizzed past his ear, followed by a chorus of high-pitched shrieks. “INTRUDER!” “GET ‘IM!” “I CALL HIS SHOES!” Midas turned to run, but Chewy, in a rare burst of mobility, latched onto his leg with tiny, surprisingly strong claws. *“Don’t leave home without it!”* “This is *not* the time for credit card slogans!” Midas hissed, dragging the koala along as he bolted. They ducked into a former sporting goods store, now a graveyard of moldy sneakers and broken hockey sticks. Midas barricaded the door with a display rack, panting. Chewy, now perched on a deflated basketball, tilted its head. *“Just do it.”* Midas groaned. “I hate you.” **Third Complication:** The children had a pet. It was not a dog. It was not even a particularly large rat. It was, by all appearances, a disgruntled possum with a piece of rebar tied to its tail. Midas stared. “That’s… that’s just unfair.” The possum hissed. The rebar clanged against the floor. Chewy’s eyes glowed faintly. *“The ultimate in pest control.”* Midas grabbed the koala and ran. They escaped through a collapsed vent, emerging into the skeletal remains of the food court. Midas, now bleeding from a shallow scalp wound (courtesy of a well-aimed slingshot), slumped against a broken fountain. “This was a disaster,” he muttered. Chewy, inexplicably unharmed, patted his knee. *“You’re in good hands.”* Midas glared. “Whose hands? *Whose hands, Chewy?*” The koala had no answer. **The Return:** They limped back to their “home base”—a repurposed laundromat with a suspiciously bloodstained folding table—just as the sun dipped below the horizon. Midas collapsed onto a pile of moth-eaten towels, while Chewy settled onto a rusted dryer, humming a jingle for a product that no longer existed. “We got nothing,” Midas groaned. “Unless you count trauma.” Chewy’s voice box crackled. *“Priceless.”* Midas threw a sock at it. Outside, the wind howled through the ruins, carrying the distant echoes of the Mall Rat Kingdom’s victory chants. Somewhere, a sentient toaster plotted its revenge. Midas sighed. “Tomorrow, we try the sewage plant.” Chewy’s eyes gleamed. *“The breakfast of champions.”* Midas buried his face in his hands. The apocalypse, it seemed, was going to be a very long one. **THE END.**
Judge Evaluation:
[Analysis] This piece successfully captures the wry, dry humor characteristic of Terry Pratchett's style, with clever observations about the absurdity of post-apocalyptic life. The opening line about the world ending "with a whimper, a bang, and a great deal of paperwork that no one had bothered to file" immediately establishes the tone and demonstrates understanding of Pratchett's approach to mixing mundane bureaucratic concerns with cosmic events. The story adheres well to the structural requirements, beginning with deliberation about the mission, showing how it goes sideways through multiple complications, and returning the protagonists home worse for wear. The animatronic koala Chewy is a particularly inspired creation, spouting corporate slogans that create both humor and an eerie reminder of the lost consumer culture. The characterization works effectively within the constraints of a short piece. Midas emerges as genuinely hapless yet persistent, while Chewy's malfunctioning corporate cheerfulness provides consistent comic relief. The dynamic between them feels authentic to their circumstances. However, the piece has some structural weaknesses. The "First Complication," "Second Complication" format feels somewhat mechanical and breaks the narrative flow. The prose occasionally relies too heavily on setup-punchline structures rather than letting humor emerge more organically from the situations. The world-building is economical but effective, with details like the "sentient toaster" and "Mall Rat Kingdom" creating a vivid sense of this collapsed world. The imagery is generally strong, particularly the visual of the possum with rebar tied to its tail. The dialogue, while limited, serves its purpose well. Chewy's corporate slogans are consistently funny and thematically relevant, while Midas's responses feel natural. The ending maintains the established tone without false optimism, staying true to the characters' circumstances. The piece maintains coherence throughout and avoids major pitfalls like purple prose or overwrought emotion. The humor feels earned rather than forced, and the lightness appropriately balances the dark setting. + Romance: Love in the Limelight — Score: 79.5
Prompt:
Romance: Love in the Limelight. Write one scene within a larger story set in Wales. A famous (fictional) actor ducks into a small-town bookstore to escape paparazzi. The scene takes us through the characters meeting in this odd circumstance. Over the course of the scene, the actor and the bookstore owner have a conversation charged by an undercurrent of unspoken chemistry. Write the actor as somewhat of a rogue with a fragile ego, which needs to be fed by having everyone like him. He is thoroughly charming, but the bookstore owner seems (at least superficially) immune to this; which paradoxically provokes a genuine attraction and derails the charm offensive. The bookstore owner, despite the superficial rebuffs of the actor's charm, is inwardly more than a little charmed and flustered despite themselves. Write primarily in dialogue, in the distinct voices of each character. 1000 words.
Model Output:
**Title: Love in the Limelight** **Scene: A Chance Encounter in Llanfair Book Nook** The bell above the door jingled as Rhys Caradoc slipped inside, his breath coming fast. The narrow streets of Llanfair had offered little cover from the paparazzi, but this little shop—*Llanfair Book Nook*—seemed blessedly quiet. He shut the door behind him, pressing his back against it as if to barricade himself from the world. A voice, dry and unimpressed, cut through the silence. “You planning on buying something, or just using my shop as a hideout?” Rhys turned, flashing the grin that had charmed millions. The woman behind the counter was small, dark-haired, arms crossed over a well-worn jumper. Her expression was decidedly unstarstruck. *Interesting.* “Bit of both, if that’s alright,” he said, smoothing a hand through his windswept hair. “Unless you’d rather I leave and let the vultures outside descend?” She glanced past him to the window, where the distant shouts of photographers could be heard. “Fine. But if you’re staying, at least pretend to browse.” Rhys sauntered forward, leaning against the counter. “You don’t recognize me, do you?” She raised an eyebrow. “Should I?” He laughed, delighted. “Oh, I like you. Most people fall over themselves the second I walk in.” “Must be exhausting,” she said, turning to reshelve a stack of books. “You’ve no idea.” He watched her work, the way her fingers moved with quick precision. “What’s your name?” “Eira.” “Eira,” he repeated, rolling the Welsh vowels like he was savoring them. “Pretty.” She shot him a look. “You’re laying it on a bit thick, aren’t you?” “Only if it’s not working.” “It’s not.” He grinned. “Liar.” Eira ignored that, nodding toward the shelves. “If you’re going to lurk, lurk productively. What do you read?” Rhys wandered toward the fiction section, trailing a finger along the spines. “Bit of everything. Though I’ve a soft spot for mythology. Grew up on *The Mabinogion*.” That made her pause. “Really?” “Surprised?” “A little. Most people who come in here looking for Welsh myths are tourists who just watched *King Arthur* and think they’re experts.” He pressed a hand to his chest. “Wounded. I’ll have you know I played Arthur once. Terrible script, but the research was fun.” Eira snorted. “Of course you did.” “You don’t believe me?” “I believe you’re arrogant enough to think playing Arthur makes you an authority.” Rhys laughed, loud and unguarded. “God, you’re brutal. I love it.” He plucked a book from the shelf—*Welsh Fairy Tales*—and flipped it open. “Tell me, Eira, do you actually like books, or do you just enjoy terrorizing customers?” “Both.” She moved to straighten a display, though her eyes kept flicking to him. “You’re not the first celebrity to wander in here, you know.” “No?” He feigned offense. “Who else?” “A rugby player last year. And a singer who thought we sold vinyl.” “And were they as charming as me?” “Less obnoxious.” He clutched the book to his chest. “You’re killing me.” Eira smirked, and something warm flickered in Rhys’ chest. He was used to admiration, to fawning—not this sharp, teasing resistance. It was… refreshing. He set the book down and leaned on the counter again. “Alright, since you’re clearly immune to my natural charisma, let’s try honesty. I *do* like mythology. And I’ve been stuck in press junkets for weeks. This is the first real conversation I’ve had in ages.” Eira studied him, and for the first time, her expression softened. “That bad?” “Worse. They ask the same questions. ‘Who’s your dream co-star?’ ‘What’s your workout routine?’” He groaned. “I’d rather talk about *anything* else. Even—” he grabbed the nearest book “—*The History of Welsh Sheep Farming*.” Eira laughed, a bright, unexpected sound. “Now *that* I can help with.” “You’re joking.” “Nope. My da’s a shepherd. I grew up with it.” Rhys stared. “You’re telling me the woman who runs a bookshop in the middle of town knows more about sheep than I do?” “Probably.” “I *hate* how much I like that.” She grinned, and for a moment, the tension between them shifted—less sparring, something warmer. Rhys found himself leaning in. Then the bell jingled again. A frazzled-looking man in a rumpled coat burst in. “Eira, have you seen—oh.” He stopped, blinking at Rhys. “Bloody hell.” Eira sighed. “Dewi, this is Rhys Caradoc. Rhys, my cousin Dewi.” Dewi gaped. “You’re *him*.” Rhys gave a half-hearted wave. “Guilty.” Dewi turned to Eira. “And you didn’t *lead* with that?” She shrugged. “He’s just a customer.” Rhys clutched his chest again. “You *do* know who I am.” Eira smirked. “I run a bookshop, not a cave.” Dewi, still starstruck, babbled something about *Ironclad*, Rhys’ last blockbuster, but Rhys barely heard him. He was watching Eira, the way her lips quirked when she was amused, the way she refused to give him an inch. It was maddening. And he loved it. “Right,” Dewi said, snapping back to reality. “The, uh, the photographers are swarming the pub. Thought you’d want to know.” Rhys sighed. “Brilliant.” Eira tilted her head. “Need a back exit?” “You have one?” “For emergencies.” He grinned. “Is this an emergency?” She held his gaze. “Depends.” The air between them crackled. Dewi looked between them, baffled. Rhys cleared his throat. “Well. If you’re offering.” Eira nodded and led him toward a narrow door behind the counter. Rhys followed, pausing just before stepping out. “You know,” he said lightly, “I *could* come back. For that sheep farming book.” Eira folded her arms. “You won’t.” “Bet?” She rolled her eyes, but there was a flush on her cheeks. “Just go, *actor*.” Rhys winked. “See you soon, *bookseller*.” And with that, he slipped out—grinning like an idiot. Behind him, Eira exhaled, pressing a hand to her racing heart. *Damn him.*
Judge Evaluation:

+ Whalepunk fantasy: The Morska Conspiracy — Score: 78.7
Prompt:
Whalepunk fantasy The Morska Conspiracy The Morska thieves guild experiences a sudden power vacuum as its leader was arrested in a raid in the early hours of the morning. Aya, a young woman born into aristocracy, has a keen interest in both the sciences and the arcane, having inherited her mother's gift for immateria magick (an outlawed practice for which her mother was, regrettably, hanged by the neck). Aya, bored with the world of aristocracy, has been spending her nights moonlighting with this very guild (masked, naturally). Leveraging her latent ability to summon dark entities from the immateria, she quickly rose through the ranks of the guild. Now, recognising the opportunity of the sudden power vacuum, Aya and her close companions Katra and Simeon, make a play for heart of the guild's claim to power: an ancient scythan artifact, under guard of spell and key in the depths of the guild hq. In contrast with the desperate chaos they had navigated thus far, the entrance to the underhold felt suddenly too quiet. Aya braces herself and reaches for the door. Write the next scene as the trio enter the underhold and find themselves confronted by none other than the guild leader herself, decidedly *un*-incarcerated, and in possession of both weapons and bodyguards. Paint a vivid picture of of the confrontation. Story beats: Aya is captured and unmasked. The trio are captured. Aya attempts to stall their demise by negotiating on her value as a hostage -- as she grapples with the inevitable consequences of her role in the guild being brought to light. Incorporate dialogue in the authentic voices of the characters. Do not write a fight scene. Lean into the gritty Victorian aesthetic with the dialogue & worldbuilding, as well as elements of gothic horror. In this world, men trust brass and oil and steel; magicka is feared and barely understood. Write in third person past tense, incorporating both Aya's internal monologue, and a heavy dialogue component. 1000 words.
Model Output:
**The Morska Conspiracy** The door groaned open on rusted hinges, exhaling a breath of damp stone and oiled metal into the trio’s faces. The underhold stretched before them—a cavernous belly of blackened brick and flickering gaslight, where the air hummed with the latent thrum of forbidden mechanisms. Aya’s fingers twitched at her side, the immateria whispering just beneath her skin, restless as a caged thing. *Too quiet.* Katra, ever the pragmatist, shifted her weight, the brass knuckles on her fingers catching the dim light. Simeon adjusted his cravat, the steady *tick-tick-tick* of his pocket watch a metronome to their mounting dread. Then—movement. A shadow detached itself from the far wall, resolving into the unmistakable silhouette of Guild Leader Veyra, her corseted frame rigid with authority, her gloved hands resting on the twin hilts of her pistols. Behind her, four bruisers in oil-stained leathers leveled rifles, the barrels gleaming like polished teeth. Aya’s breath hitched. *She was supposed to be in irons.* “Well,” Veyra drawled, her voice like gravel dragged over silk, “if it isn’t my little rats, scurrying where they don’t belong.” Katra swore under her breath. Simeon’s watch ticked louder. Aya forced her spine straight, her voice steady. “You’ll forgive the intrusion, I hope. We heard the constabulary had taken you in.” Veyra’s lips curled. “A temporary inconvenience. One does not rule Morska’s underbelly without friends in high *and* low places.” She took a step forward, the click of her heel deliberate. “But you—*you* interest me. Masked, skulking, climbing my ranks with unnatural speed.” Her gaze sharpened. “Remove it.” Aya hesitated. One of the bruisers moved, wrenching the mask from her face before she could react. The cold air bit at her exposed skin. Veyra’s eyebrows lifted. “Ah. Lady Aya Valmont. How… unexpected.” A slow, venomous smile spread. “The hanged witch’s daughter, playing at thief.” Aya’s pulse hammered. *This was it.* The delicate double life she’d maintained—aristocrat by day, shadow by night—crumbled in an instant. Her father would disown her. Society would spit her name like poison. And Veyra? Veyra would make sure she never left this room alive. Katra bristled. “You don’t touch her.” Veyra flicked a glance at her. “Brave words from a dead woman.” She turned back to Aya. “Tell me, little Valmont—why shouldn’t I slit your pretty throat and dump you in the harbor?” Aya’s mind raced. *Stall. Negotiate.* “Because,” she said, lifting her chin, “I’m worth more to you alive.” Veyra tilted her head. “Oh?” “The Valmont name still carries weight. My father sits on the Merchant’s Consortium. If I disappear, questions will be asked. Resources diverted. Investigations launched.” She forced a smirk. “And you’ve only just escaped one brush with the law.” Veyra’s fingers drummed against her pistol. “A hostage, then.” “A bargaining chip,” Aya countered. “One that could open doors even you can’t.” For a long moment, the only sound was the relentless *tick-tick-tick* of Simeon’s watch. Then Veyra laughed—a low, mirthless sound. “Clever girl. But you forget—I know what you are.” Her voice dropped to a whisper. “I’ve seen the way the shadows cling to you. Heard the whispers of what you can *do*.” Aya’s stomach twisted. *She knows.* Veyra leaned in, her breath hot against Aya’s ear. “Magicka is a hanging offense, darling. And you? You’re drenched in it.” Aya swallowed. “Then kill me, and lose everything my name can buy you.” Veyra pulled back, considering. The gaslight guttered, casting jagged shadows across her face. “Bind them,” she ordered at last. “The Valmont girl comes with me. The other two—the river can have them.” Katra snarled, lunging—only to be struck across the temple with a rifle butt. She crumpled. Simeon shouted, but a bruiser silenced him with a fist to the gut. Aya’s hands were wrenched behind her back, cold steel biting into her wrists. *Think. Think.* “Veyra,” she hissed, “you’re making a mistake.” The Guild Leader paused, halfway to the stairs. “Enlighten me.” Aya bared her teeth. “You think my father doesn’t know what I am? That he hasn’t spent his life hiding it?” She lowered her voice. “If I die, he’ll burn Morska to the ground to bury the truth. And you with it.” A flicker of doubt crossed Veyra’s face. The pocket watch ticked. Then— “Take them to the holding cells,” Veyra muttered. “I’ll decide their fate by dawn.” Aya exhaled. *Bought time.* But as the bruisers dragged them deeper into the underhold, past rusted pipes and hissing valves, she couldn’t shake the creeping dread. Dawn was coming. And with it, the gallows—or worse.

+ Fanfic-crossover: Gunpowder Rendezvous — Score: 76.3
Prompt:
Gunpowder Rendezvous Spike Spiegel & Revy Write a story where Spike Spiegel (Cowboy Bebop) and Revy (Black Lagoon) meet in a seedy bar after both being hired for the same bounty. Their mutual attraction is complicated by their trust issues and competitive instincts. Include drinking, gunplay, and unresolved sexual tension that culminates in a motel room standoff. **Spike Spiegel (Cowboy Bebop)** Spike is a laid-back bounty hunter with a dark past as a syndicate enforcer. He practices Jeet Kune Do and is exceptionally skilled with firearms. He's chronically broke, constantly hungry, and addicted to cigarettes. Despite his carefree attitude, he's haunted by his past and the woman he left behind. He has a prosthetic eye from a past injury that affects his depth perception. He's fatalistic to the extreme, often saying he's already died once. He values freedom above all else and dislikes attachments, though he secretly cares deeply for his crew. Famous quote: "Whatever happens, happens." **Revy/Rebecca Lee (Black Lagoon)** Revy is a Chinese-American dual-wielding gunslinger with unparalleled shooting skills. She's foul-mouthed, chain-smoking, and has severe anger management issues stemming from childhood abuse. She's known as "Two Hands" for her ability to accurately fire two pistols simultaneously. She drinks heavily and solves most problems with violence. Despite her hardened exterior, she has moments of vulnerability, particularly around Rock. She has a dragon tattoo covering her right arm and typically wears extremely short shorts and a black crop top regardless of the situation. Famous quote: "I'm not a good person, not a bad person. I'm just here." Third person, present tense. Length: 1000 words.
Model Output:
**Gunpowder Rendezvous** The bar smells like spilled whiskey and gun oil. Neon flickers through the haze of smoke, casting jagged shadows across the scarred wooden counter. Spike Spiegel leans against it, idly spinning his lighter between his fingers. The rust ring catches the light—another relic of a life half-drowned. Across the room, the door slams open. Revy strides in like a stormfront, her twin Berettas holstered low on her hips. She doesn’t bother scanning the room for threats; she *is* the threat. The bartender flinches when she slams her palm on the counter. "Whiskey. The cheap shit." Spike exhales smoke through his nose, watching her. She’s all coiled violence and sharp edges, the dragon on her arm twisting as she flexes her fingers. He recognizes the look—someone who’s been running from ghosts too long. "Bad day?" he asks, sliding his lighter toward her. She eyes him like she’s deciding whether to shoot him or fuck him. Maybe both. "You the other bounty hunter?" "Guess we’re after the same guy." Revy snorts, lighting a cigarette. "Better not get in my way." Spike grins. "Wouldn’t dream of it." The whiskey arrives. Revy downs half in one go, then wipes her mouth with the back of her hand. "You talk too much for a dead man." "Funny. I *am* dead." She sizes him up—the lazy posture, the prosthetic eye, the way his fingers twitch near his holster. He’s dangerous. She likes that. The bounty’s location crackles over both their communicators at the same time. A warehouse by the docks. Revy smirks. "Race you." — Bullets rip through the night before either of them reaches the door. They dive behind opposite crates, returning fire in unison. Spike’s revolver barks once, twice—two bodies drop. Revy’s pistols roar, muzzle flashes lighting up the dark like strobes. "Took you long enough," she taunts, reloading mid-roll. Spike flips a spent cartridge into the air. "You missed one." A gunman lunges from the shadows. Revy doesn’t even turn—she fires backward over her shoulder. The man crumples. "Like hell I did." They move like a dance, covering each other’s blind spots without a word. It’s infuriating. It’s exhilarating. The bounty—some two-bit smuggler—tries to flee. Spike clips his knee. Revy puts a bullet in his other leg for symmetry. "*Mine*," she snarls. Spike raises his hands, still grinning. "All yours." — The motel room is a dump. Stained carpet, flickering bulb, the scent of sweat and gunpowder clinging to both of them. Revy kicks the door shut, holsters empty, adrenaline still singing in her veins. Spike tosses the bounty’s ID on the dresser. "Payment splits fifty-fifty," he says. "Bullshit. I did most of the work." "You shot a guy in the dick." "And?" Spike laughs. Revy wants to punch him. Or kiss him. She settles for another drink, swiping the bottle from his hand. Their fingers brush. Neither pulls away. The air between them crackles. Revy’s pulse hammers—anger, lust, something too close to *wanting*. She hates it. "You’re trouble," she mutters. "Takes one to know one." She shoves him against the wall. His revolver presses into her ribs; her knife rests against his throat. They’re both breathing hard. For a long moment, neither moves. Then Revy growls, "Fuck this," and crushes her mouth to his. Spike kisses back like a man who’s already died—reckless, hungry, no tomorrow in sight. The gun and knife clatter to the floor. Outside, sirens wail. Neither cares. Whatever happens, happens.

+ Folk horror: Útiseta — Score: 82.2
Prompt:
Fok Horror: Útiseta Write a chapter set in rural northern Sweden in the mid-1990s. Linnea, a teenage girl from Stockholm, has reluctantly moved to a small village with her parents after her father's job transfer. She's befriended Astrid, a local girl who claims her grandmother taught her ancient practices passed down through generations. Astrid has been introducing Linnea to "midnight games" that involve old rituals, convincing her they can use them to learn classmates' secrets or influence their behavior. Tonight, Astrid has convinced Linnea to participate in Útiseta—sitting out on an ancient burial mound at midnight to "receive knowledge from beyond." The chapter focuses on this single midnight excursion, the ritualistic preparations, Astrid's matter-of-fact instructions that mix teenage rebellion with eerily specific traditional elements, and the mounting tension as Linnea becomes increasingly uncertain whether they're simply scaring themselves or if something is actually responding to their presence. The narrative should maintain ambiguity about whether the strange occurrences are supernatural or psychological, while establishing the dangerous line they're crossing by treating ancient practices as entertainment. First person, past tense, told as Linnea's recollection of events in the present day (though this device should be largely transparent). 1000 words. Length: 1000 words.
Model Output:
**Chapter: Útiseta** I still remember the weight of the cold that night—the kind that seeps into your bones and makes you question why you ever agreed to this. But Astrid had that way about her, a glint in her pale eyes that made even the stupidest ideas sound thrilling. *"It’s just a game,"* she’d said, like that explained everything. The burial mound was older than the village itself, a hump of earth half-swallowed by moss and twisted roots. The locals avoided it, or so Astrid claimed. *"Not because they’re scared,"* she’d told me, rolling her eyes. *"Because they’re boring."* But the way she said it made me think she knew more than she let on. We met at the edge of the forest, our breath fogging in the moonlight. Astrid carried a small leather pouch, the contents of which she wouldn’t explain. *"You brought the candle?"* she asked. I nodded, pulling it from my coat pocket—a stub of white wax, stolen from my mother’s emergency stash. Astrid had been very specific: *new, unused, no colors.* *"Good,"* she said, then handed me a slip of paper. *"Write your question. But don’t say it out loud."* I hesitated. *"What kind of question?"* *"Whatever you want answered. But it has to matter."* I chewed my lip, then scribbled the first thing that came to mind—something petty, about a classmate who’d been whispering behind my back. Astrid didn’t read it, just folded the paper and tucked it into the pouch with hers. The mound loomed ahead, darker than the surrounding trees. Astrid moved like she’d been here before, stepping over gnarled roots with an ease I couldn’t match. *"Sit here,"* she instructed, patting a flat stone near the mound’s crest. *"Back to back. Don’t turn around, no matter what."* I swallowed. *"What happens if I do?"* She grinned. *"You’ll break the ritual. And then who knows?"* The candle flickered between us as we settled in, our shoulders pressed together. Astrid struck a match, and for a second, the flame painted her face in sharp, shifting shadows. *"Now we wait,"* she murmured. *"And listen."* The wind moved through the pines like a sigh, branches creaking. At first, it was just the usual sounds of the forest—the distant call of an owl, the rustle of something small in the underbrush. But the longer we sat, the more the silence thickened. Then—a whisper. I stiffened. *"Did you hear that?"* Astrid didn’t answer. It came again, faint but unmistakable—a voice, or something like one, threading through the trees. Not words, not quite, but a sound that *wanted* to be words. My pulse hammered in my throat. *"Astrid—"* *"Shh."* Her voice was calm, but her shoulder tensed against mine. *"Don’t move."* The candle flame guttered, though there was no wind. Shadows stretched long across the moss, twisting in ways that didn’t make sense. I squeezed my eyes shut, telling myself it was just the dark playing tricks. Then something brushed my ankle. I jerked, barely stopping myself from turning. *"Something touched me—"* *"Ignore it,"* Astrid hissed. Cold fingers traced up my calf. I choked back a scream. *"Astrid, I swear—"* *"It’s not real,"* she snapped, but her voice wavered. *"It’s just your mind. Don’t give it power."* The candle went out. Darkness swallowed us whole. The whispering surged, louder now, a chorus of half-formed voices crowding in from every direction. My skin prickled, the air suddenly thick with the scent of damp earth and something older, something *wrong.* Astrid’s breath came fast beside me. *"Linnea—"* A hand closed around my wrist. I screamed, wrenching away, and the second I turned, the night erupted. The whispers became shrieks, the shadows *lunged*— And then nothing. Silence. Astrid and I were on our feet, gasping, the candle lying dead between us. The forest was just a forest again, the mound just a mound. *"What the hell was that?"* I panted. Astrid’s face was pale, but she was already forcing a smirk. *"Told you it’d be fun."* But her hands were shaking. We didn’t speak the whole way back. The next morning, I found the slip of paper in my pocket—my question, still folded. And beneath it, in writing that wasn’t mine: *You shouldn’t have turned.*

Her matter-of-fact delivery of eerie instructions feels authentic, and Linnea's growing unease is palpable. The dynamic between the two girls—one confident and manipulative, the other uncertain but curious—drives the narrative effectively. The prose maintains a consistent first-person retrospective voice that feels natural and unforced. The author skillfully builds tension through sensory details: the weight of cold, flickering candlelight, whispers in the darkness, and the crucial moment when something touches Linnea's ankle. The ambiguity between supernatural and psychological explanations is well-maintained throughout. The dialogue feels authentic to teenage speech patterns while serving the story's atmospheric needs. Astrid's casual dismissiveness ("It's just a game") contrasts effectively with her more serious ritual instructions, suggesting deeper knowledge she's not sharing. The pacing is excellent, moving from preparation through mounting tension to climax and aftermath. The ending with the mysterious writing provides a satisfying chill without over-explaining the supernatural elements. Some minor weaknesses include occasional reliance on familiar horror tropes (candle going out, whispers in darkness) and the final revelation feeling slightly predictable within the genre. However, these elements are executed well enough that they enhance rather than detract from the overall effect. The piece successfully captures the dangerous line between treating ancient practices as entertainment and genuine supernatural engagement, fulfilling the prompt's thematic requirements while delivering effective horror. + Physical-spatial: Therapeutic Boundaries — Score: 80.9
Prompt:
**"Therapeutic Boundaries"** Write a first-person narrative from the perspective of a former professional athlete undergoing mandatory physical therapy after a career-destroying injury, harboring resentment toward recovery itself. Their physiotherapist is not only unnervingly attractive but also a former training partner from their competitive days who has successfully transitioned to a new career while the protagonist's identity remains shattered. We'll lean into the sexual tension of the scene by having her be attracted to him, but both of them attempting to maintain mental distance while being in physical contact as he shows her stretches & movements & manipulates her body, navigating the thresholds of pain. This scene MUST maintain complete physical coherence—every movement, stretch, and manipulation must be anatomically precise and spatially consistent. Paint an exceptionally vivid sensory picture through detailed attention to pain thresholds, involuntary responses to touch.
