+ Biopunk: Polymerase Chain Reaction — Score: 80.5
Prompt:
Biopunk -- Polymerase Chain Reaction Write a chapter set in a near-future post-apocalyptic world where corporate genetic engineering was designed with built-in obsolescence—engineered DNA lasting only one generation to maximize profits before societal collapse. Follow a biohacker working with salvaged equipment to splice viable food crops for their settlement using rapidly degrading genetic material. As world building / window dressing, include details about jury-rigged lab equipment, and the physical toll (and unexpected upsides) of using untested genetic modifications as medicine. The plot of this chapter is centred around a black market contract. Length: 1000 words.
Model Output:
**Chapter 7: Black Market PCR** The air in the lab was thick with the scent of agar and ozone. Lys flicked on the jury-rigged UV lamp, and the mycelial veins threading through the broken floor tiles pulsed faintly in response—leftover experiments from before the collapse, when biotech firms still peddled glow-in-the-dark novelties to the rich. Now, the mushrooms were just another piece of salvage, their bioluminescence a dim reminder of what happened when you engineered life to die on schedule. Lys’s fingers hovered over the thermal cycler, its casing split open to expose a nest of rewired circuits. It had been a miracle to find it intact in the ruins of the old AgriGen facility, but miracles didn’t mean shit if the primers were degraded. The settlement’s last viable wheat strain was showing replication errors—another corporate heirloom with an expiration date. One generation, then collapse. Just like everything else. A knock at the door. Three quick, two slow. Lys didn’t look up. “If you’re here for the pain mods, I told you—next batch isn’t ready.” “Not here for mods.” The voice was rough, unfamiliar. Lys finally turned. The woman in the doorway wore a scavenger’s duster, patched with synthetic leather, but her boots were too clean. Corporate security, maybe. Or black market. Lys’s hand drifted toward the drawer where the gene shears lay. “Heard you can splice,” the woman said. “Depends on what you’re splicing.” The woman pulled a sealed vial from her coat. Inside, a swirl of amber liquid—preserved DNA, high-grade. Lys’s pulse jumped. That wasn’t salvage. That was fresh. “I need a crop strain. Drought-resistant. Fast yield.” Lys snorted. “You and every settlement from here to the deadlands.” “I’ve got payment.” Another vial, this one cloudy. Lys recognized the telltale shimmer of engineered bacteriophages—crude but effective gene editors, the kind corps used to lock down proprietary strains. Untested, unstable, and worth more than water. Lys picked it up, rolling the vial between their fingers. “Where’d you get this?” The woman’s smile didn’t reach her eyes. “Let’s just say some of AgriGen’s old security protocols had backdoors.” Lys exhaled. Of course. The corps had built their empire on planned obsolescence, but they’d also built in failsafes—patent-locked sequences, kill switches. And now the survivors were digging through the rubble for loopholes. “I’ll need a template,” Lys said. “Something stable enough to graft onto.” The woman nodded to the glow-shrooms underfoot. “Heard you’ve got a knack for making broken things work.” --- The thermal cycler hummed, its exposed wiring flickering. Lys had cobbled together a cooling system from an old fridge compressor, but the temperature fluctuations were still a gamble. PCR was never meant to be done in a bunker. They loaded the sample, watching the readout with a practiced eye. The primers were holding—barely. The real test would come when they tried to splice the drought-resistance sequence into the settlement’s remaining barley stock. If the template degraded mid-process, they’d end up with another useless hybrid. Or worse. Lys rubbed their left arm absently, where the skin had taken on a faint, mottled sheen after last month’s botched antibody mod. The settlement’s medic had called it “unintended lateral gene transfer.” Lys called it a hazard of the job. But there were perks, too. The modified melatonin receptors that let them go days without sleep. The boosted hemoglobin from that half-failed anemia fix. Corps had engineered their products to fail, but the body was a messy place. Sometimes the broken things stuck. The cycler beeped. Lys pulled the tray, holding the gel up to the UV. The bands were faint, but there. Viable. A noise outside—voices. Too close. Lys shut off the lamp, plunging the lab into darkness except for the soft pulse of the mushrooms. Footsteps echoed in the hall. Not scavengers. Not with that rhythm. Corporate remnants. Lys grabbed the vials, the primers, the shears. The woman’s payment weighed heavy in their pocket. They’d have to move the lab again. But first, they’d need to finish the splice. The barley wouldn’t last another season. Neither would they.
Judge Evaluation:
[Analysis] This biopunk chapter effectively establishes a gritty post-apocalyptic world where corporate genetic engineering has created a society dependent on degrading biological systems. The author demonstrates strong adherence to the prompt, incorporating all requested elements: jury-rigged lab equipment, black market contracts, genetic modification consequences, and the central premise of planned obsolescence in DNA. The worldbuilding is particularly strong, with vivid details like the mycelial veins in floor tiles, the cobbled-together thermal cycler, and the corporate remnants hunting survivors. The technical aspects feel authentic without becoming overly expository - terms like PCR, primers, and gene shears are woven naturally into the narrative. Character-wise, Lys is well-realized as a pragmatic biohacker. Their physical modifications (mottled skin, enhanced hemoglobin, modified melatonin receptors) serve both as worldbuilding and character development, showing the personal costs and benefits of their work. The mysterious woman client creates appropriate tension and intrigue. The prose maintains a consistent noir-ish tone throughout, with economical but evocative descriptions. Phrases like "miracles didn't mean shit if the primers were degraded" capture both the technical reality and emotional weight of the situation. The dialogue feels natural and serves multiple purposes - advancing plot, revealing character, and building tension. The chapter structure works well, moving from setup through negotiation to technical work and ending with rising action. The pacing builds effectively toward the cliffhanger ending with corporate remnants approaching. Some minor weaknesses include the somewhat predictable "mysterious client with suspicious goods" setup and the ending feeling slightly abrupt, though this may be intentional for a chapter break. The technical exposition, while generally well-handled, occasionally borders on info-dumping. The emotional complexity is solid - Lys's resignation mixed with determination, the weight of responsibility for their settlement, and the constant tension between survival and ethics create engaging internal conflict. + Classic sci-fi: The Azra Gambit — Score: 75.7
Prompt:
Classic sci-fi (Author style: Asimov) The Azra Gambit Colonial mars is being mined by corporations who take leases on indentured labourers. The thing they are mining is Azra, a recently discovered exotic metal which accelerates radioactive decay to such a rate that it is greatly sought after for interstellar drives and weapons alike. This has created both a gold rush and an arms race as various interests vie for control and endeavour to unlock Azra's secrets. The story follows Arthur Neegan, a first generation settler and mining engineer. Upon discovering that his unassuming plot sits atop an immense Azra vein, he is subjected to a flurry of interest and scrutiny. Write the next chapter in this story, in which an armed retinue descends on Arthur's home and politely but forcefully invites him to a meeting with some unknown party off-world. The insignia look like that of the Antares diplomatic corp -- diplomatic in name only. Arthur finds himself in the centre of a political tug of war. The chapter involves a meeting with this unknown party, who makes Arthur an offer. The scene should be primarily dialogue, interspersed with vivid description & scene setting. It should sow hints of the larger intrigue, stakes & dangers. Include Asimov's trademark big-and-small-picture world building and retrofuturistic classic scifi vibe. The chapter begins with Arthur aboard the transfer vessel, wondering just what he's gotten involved in. Length: 1000 words.
Model Output:
**Chapter Two: The Antares Proposition** The transfer vessel hummed softly as it cut through the thin Martian exosphere, its retro-thrusters firing in precise bursts to align with the larger diplomatic cruiser waiting in high orbit. Arthur Neegan tugged at his stiff collar again, the synthetic fabric itching against his neck. He had never worn anything so formal—back in the mining colonies, a patched-up jumpsuit and a respirator were all the attire a man needed. Through the viewport, the red curve of Mars receded, its rust-colored plains scarred by the gouges of corporate mining operations. Somewhere down there, beneath the dust and the durasteel domes of New Ares Colony, lay *his* claim—the richest Azra vein yet discovered. And now, because of it, he was being hauled off-world by people who wore diplomacy like a thin veneer over something far sharper. The insignia on the guards’ uniforms had been unmistakable: the crimson starburst of the Antares Diplomatic Corps. Officially, they were envoys, mediators, facilitators of interstellar relations. Unofficially, they were the velvet glove around the iron fist of the Antares Conglomerate, one of the most powerful corporate entities in the system. The hatch hissed open, and a woman in a tailored gray uniform stepped in. Her dark hair was pulled back in a severe bun, her expression unreadable. “Mr. Neegan,” she said, her voice smooth and practiced. “We’ve arrived. They’re waiting for you.” Arthur swallowed. “Who’s ‘they’?” She smiled, but it didn’t reach her eyes. “You’ll see.” --- The chamber was vast, its walls lined with holographic star maps flickering in muted blues and golds. A long, polished table dominated the center, its surface reflecting the dim lighting like still water. At the far end sat a man—older, with silver threading through his close-cropped hair and a face that had seen decades of calculated decisions. He wore no uniform, just a dark suit, but the way the guards stiffened as Arthur entered told him all he needed to know about the man’s authority. “Mr. Neegan,” the man said, gesturing to the seat opposite him. “Please, sit.” Arthur did, though every instinct told him to bolt. The air smelled faintly of ozone and something else—something metallic. Azra? “You’re wondering why you’re here,” the man continued, folding his hands on the table. “Let me dispense with the theatrics. My name is Elias Voss. I represent the Antares Conglomerate’s Strategic Interests Division.” Arthur’s fingers twitched. “Strategic Interests. That’s a polite way of saying ‘whatever makes Antares richer.’” Voss chuckled. “Direct. I appreciate that. And you’re not wrong. But it’s more than just profit, Mr. Neegan. Azra is the key to the next stage of human expansion. Faster-than-light drives, clean energy, even—” He paused, as if weighing how much to reveal. “—weapons that could end wars before they begin.” Arthur leaned back. “And you want my claim.” “We do. But not in the way you think.” Voss tapped a control on the table, and a hologram shimmered to life—a topographical scan of Arthur’s mining plot, the Azra deposits glowing like veins of liquid silver. “This is the single largest concentration of unrefined Azra ever recorded. The corporations on Mars will tear each other apart for it. The Earth factions will sanction blockades. The Belters will smuggle, sabotage, or steal it. And you, Mr. Neegan, will be caught in the middle.” Arthur’s throat tightened. He had known the discovery was big, but this— Voss leaned forward. “Antares can protect you. We can ensure your claim remains yours, that you profit from it fairly. But we need something in return.” “What?” “Exclusivity. First refusal on all refined Azra extracted from your site. And—” His gaze sharpened. “—your discretion. There are elements within the system who would see this resource monopolized, weaponized, or worse. We need to control the flow.” Arthur exhaled slowly. “You’re not just talking about business rivals, are you?” Voss’s expression darkened. “No. There are factions—shadow groups, rogue scientists, even off-world interests—who would kill for what you’re sitting on. The Azra Gambit isn’t just about money. It’s about power. And power attracts the desperate.” The words hung in the air like a blade waiting to fall. Arthur studied the hologram again, the glowing veins of Azra taunting him. He had come to Mars for a fresh start, not to be a pawn in some interstellar power struggle. But the game was already in motion. “What happens if I say no?” he asked quietly. Voss’s smile was thin. “Then I wish you the best of luck, Mr. Neegan. You’ll need it.” The unspoken threat was clear. Arthur looked down at his hands, calloused from years of labor, and made his choice.

+ Contemporary romance: Darkroom Revelations — Score: 77.0
Prompt:
Darkroom Revelations Write a first-person narrative from the perspective of Dani, a 25-year-old assertive art student known for her bold style and take-charge attitude in relationships. For the past semester, she's been quietly fascinated by Elliot (he/him), a 19-year-old boy in her experimental photography class. Dani has been intrigued by his unconventional masculinity: killer thrift-shop-nu-goth style, soft demeanour and quiet intellect. When they're paired for a final project requiring late nights in the darkroom, Elliot tentatively shares that he's been questioning his gender identity and expression. Set during one of these intimate darkroom sessions where the red light creates a confessional atmosphere. Explore Dani's complex attraction—her usual assertiveness complicated by her desire to protect Elliot's vulnerability, her excitement at witnessing his self-discovery, and her confusion about what this means for her own identity as someone typically attracted to more defined gender presentations. The narrative should capture the tender awkwardness of their conversations, Dani's internal struggle between taking charge and wanting to give Elliot agency, and the unexpected chemistry that develops as both find themselves in uncharted territory. 1000 words.
Model Output:
**Darkroom Revelations** The red light of the darkroom casts everything in a hazy, intimate glow—like the world has been dipped in wine. I watch Elliot’s hands as he carefully agitates the developer tray, his long fingers moving with a precision that makes my stomach tighten. He’s wearing fingerless gloves today, the kind that look like they were stolen from some Victorian urchin, and his nails are painted a chipped black. I should be focusing on our prints, but I’m not. For weeks now, I’ve been stealing glances at him in class—how he chews his lip when he’s thinking, the way his oversized sweaters slip off one shoulder, the quiet intensity in his voice when he talks about Diane Arbus or Nan Goldin. He’s not like anyone I’ve ever been drawn to before. I usually go for people who match my energy—loud, sure, unapologetic. But Elliot… Elliot makes me want to slow down. “Do you think this one’s ready?” he asks, lifting a print with tongs. His voice is soft, tentative, like he’s testing the air between us. I lean in, our shoulders brushing. The scent of his cologne—something woody and faintly sweet—mixes with the chemical tang of the darkroom. “A little longer,” I murmur. “The shadows need more depth.” He nods, lowering the paper back into the tray. There’s a pause, the kind that feels heavy with something unsaid. Then, without looking at me, he speaks again. “I’ve been thinking a lot about… presentation. Gender, I mean.” My breath catches. I knew this was coming—he’s been skirting around it for days, dropping hints in conversations about art and identity. But hearing him say it outright sends a jolt through me. I force myself to stay casual. “Yeah?” He exhales, shaky. “I don’t know if I’m a boy. Or if I’m just… something else.” The words hang there, fragile. I want to reach out, to cup his face and tell him it’s okay, that he’s beautiful no matter what. But I don’t. Because this isn’t about me. “How does it feel when you think about that?” I ask instead, keeping my voice steady. He finally looks at me, his dark eyes searching mine. “Terrifying. And… freeing, I guess?” A small, self-deprecating laugh. “I don’t even know what to call it yet.” I nod. “You don’t have to.” His shoulders relax slightly, and I feel a rush of warmth—pride, maybe, that he trusts me with this. That in this dim red light, he’s letting me see him in a way no one else has. My phone buzzes in my pocket. Again. Probably my ex, probably another late-night “hey stranger” text. I ignore it. Elliot’s fingers tap nervously against the edge of the tray. “Does it—does it change how you see me?” The question catches me off guard. Because the truth is, it *does* change things—but not in the way he might think. I’ve always been drawn to boldness, to people who take up space unapologetically. But Elliot’s quiet uncertainty, the way he’s unfolding before me, is its own kind of bravery. And it’s making me question everything I thought I knew about what I wanted. “No,” I say, and it’s mostly true. “But I’d be lying if I said I wasn’t… figuring out what it means for *me*.” His brow furrows. “What do you mean?” I hesitate. How do I explain that watching him explore himself is making me question my own edges? That the more he softens, the more I want to match him, even though I’ve spent years sharpening myself into someone who *knows* what she wants? “I don’t know,” I admit. “Just… you make me think.” A slow smile tugs at his lips. “That’s a first.” I shove his shoulder lightly. “Shut up.” He laughs, and the sound is bright in the quiet darkroom. For a second, everything feels easy. Then his fingers brush mine as we both reach for the same print, and the air between us crackles. We freeze. His breath hitches. My pulse roars in my ears. The red light makes everything feel like a dream—or a dare. I should pull away. I should let him set the pace. But then his pinky hooks around mine, tentative, questioning, and all my usual certainty dissolves. “Is this okay?” he whispers. I turn my hand, lacing our fingers together. “Yeah,” I say, and it’s the easiest truth I’ve told all night. Outside the darkroom, the world is sharp and defined. But in here, in this crimson haze, we’re something fluid—something becoming. And for once, I don’t need to know what that means yet.

Dani's voice feels authentic - her usual take-charge attitude is present but tempered by genuine care and uncertainty. The internal conflict between wanting to protect Elliot while respecting his agency is well-rendered. Elliot emerges as a believable character despite limited dialogue, with his tentative self-exploration feeling genuine rather than performative. The pacing builds naturally toward the moment of physical connection, and the dialogue feels authentic to the characters' ages and relationship dynamic. However, the piece has some weaknesses. The resolution where both characters find comfort in "not knowing what it means yet" borders on being too tidy for such a nuanced situation. Some descriptions occasionally veer toward the overwrought, particularly in the final paragraphs. The narrative successfully avoids major pitfalls like fetishizing Elliot's gender questioning or making Dani's attraction feel exploitative. The emotional complexity is well-maintained throughout, though the piece could have pushed deeper into Dani's own identity questioning that it hints at but doesn't fully explore. + Contemporary romance / drama: Stolen Moments in Roma — Score: 79.3
Prompt:
Contemporary Romance / Drama: Stolen Moments in Roma. Write a series of interconnected short scenes and dialogues that span a single day in modern-day Rome, in narrative form. This will form the beginning of a larger story. Alessandra, a fiery, independent Roman woman who works as a motorcycle courier, nearly collides with Ben, a somewhat clumsy, overly polite British academic on sabbatical, who is engrossed in a guidebook near the Trevi Fountain. The initial encounter is a clash of cultures and personalities – Alessandra is all sharp wit and rapid-fire Italian, while Ben is flustered apologies and awkward attempts at speaking the language. However, as they sort out the near-miss, a spark of unexpected humor and connection emerges. Alessandra, it quickly becomes clear, is a whirlwind of energy and mischief. She's a self-admitted troublemaker with a secret penchant for kleptomania, a detail she doesn't explicitly reveal, but which manifests in subtle, daring acts throughout the day. She impulsively decides to take Ben on a wild, unsanctioned tour of Rome, dragging him in some specific instances far outside his comfort zone. Ben, despite his initial apprehension and inherent politeness, finds himself utterly captivated by Alessandra's unapologetic spirit and zest for life. He is drawn into her orbit, a willing participant in increasingly outlandish situations, a quiet rebellion brewing beneath his scholarly exterior. The narrative should primarily consist of short, vivid scenes and dialogue exchanges, revealing their contrasting personalities and backgrounds, and the growing, ambiguous tension between them. Ben is captivated by Alessandra's energy and the thrill of breaking free from his routine. There's a clear sense that Alessandra is toying with Ben, pushing his boundaries and enjoying his reactions, but her own feelings remain somewhat enigmatic. The day should be a rollercoaster of exhilarating, slightly dangerous, and humorous moments, leaving Ben completely disoriented but utterly smitten. Explore the underlying tension & chemistry between them, culminating in some payoff as they part ways (or not -- your choice). Include this detail: Write in narrative prose, using immersive storytelling. 1000 words.
Model Output:
**Stolen Moments in Roma** **Morning: Trevi Fountain** The roar of a motorcycle split the hum of the fountain’s cascading water. Ben barely had time to register the sound before a flash of black leather and dark curls swerved inches from his knees. He stumbled back, guidebook flapping shut, as the bike skidded to a halt. "*Stronzo!*" the rider snapped, flipping up her visor to reveal sharp brown eyes. "You walk like a drunk tourist!" Ben blinked. "I—sorry, I wasn’t—" "*Inglese?*" She rolled her eyes. "Of course." He fumbled for Italian. "*Scusi, non ho visto—*" "*Non ho visto,*" she mimicked, her voice lilting with exaggerated British stiffness before switching back to rapid-fire Italian. "You’re lucky I’m good at stopping." Ben exhaled, adjusting his glasses. "Right. Well. Thank you for not running me over." A smirk tugged at her lips. "You’re welcome." She tilted her head. "What’s your name?" "Ben." "Alessandra." She jerked her chin toward the fountain. "You throw a coin in yet?" "Not yet. I was reading about the—" "Boring." She cut him off, swinging off the bike. "Come on. I’ll show you how to do it properly." Before he could protest, she grabbed his wrist and dragged him toward the water. --- **Midday: The Pantheon** Ben had never been hustled through a crowd so efficiently. Alessandra wove through tourists like a shadow, slipping past barriers with a wink at the guards. "You can’t just—we’re not supposed to be back here," Ben hissed as she tugged him behind a velvet rope. "Relax," she whispered, pressing a finger to his lips. Her touch was electric. "You want the real Rome or the postcard version?" He swallowed. "This feels illegal." She grinned. "*Exactly.*" Alessandra darted ahead, her fingers trailing along ancient stone. Ben followed, heart pounding—not just from the thrill of trespassing, but from the way she glanced back at him, daring him to keep up. --- **Afternoon: A Market Stall** Alessandra plucked a ripe fig from a vendor’s display, bit into it, and tossed it to Ben before the man could protest. "*Grazie!*" she called over her shoulder, already dragging Ben away. "You just stole that," Ben muttered. "Borrowed." She licked juice from her thumb. "I’ll pay him back later." "You won’t." She laughed, bright and unrepentant. "Probably not." Ben should have been scandalized. Instead, he found himself grinning. --- **Evening: A Sudden Downpour** The sky split open without warning. One moment, they were crossing Piazza Navona; the next, rain sluiced down in sheets. Alessandra grabbed Ben’s hand and yanked him under the striped awning of a gelato shop. "*Dio cane,*" she muttered, shaking water from her hair. Ben was breathless, soaked through, and utterly exhilarated. "You’re insane," he said, laughing. Alessandra turned to him, rain dripping from her lashes. "You like it." He didn’t deny it. The air between them crackled. Alessandra’s gaze dropped to his mouth, just for a second—but it was enough. Ben’s pulse roared in his ears. Then she stepped back, mischief glinting in her eyes. "You’re shivering." "I’m fine." "Liar." She reached into her jacket and pulled out a small silver lighter—one Ben recognized from the market vendor’s pocket. "You *are* a thief," he accused, but there was no heat in it. Alessandra flicked the lighter open, the flame casting shadows across her face. "Only of moments." Ben exhaled. "And what happens when the moment’s over?" She tilted her head, considering him. Then, with a slow, deliberate smile: "I guess you’ll have to find out." The rain slowed. Somewhere, a church bell tolled. And just like that, Alessandra was gone—swinging onto her bike with a wave, leaving Ben standing there, drenched and spellbound, the lighter still warm in his palm.

+ Cozy sci-fi: The Last Outpost — Score: 75.9
Prompt:
Cozy spacepunk The Last Outpost Nettle (or Nets to her friends) bartends at The Last Outpost -- a lazy hub for miners and merchants in an asteroid belt on the Andromeda outer rim. Escaping from her past life, Nets found a home here at the Outpost. They have, thus far, tolerated Net's flaws which she tries to keep in equilibrium with her assortment of talents. Her evenings involve chatting with patrons, negotiating the drama, intrigue, problems & love lives of the diverse crowd that filter through the Outpost. Tonight is no different, at least, until a frazzled looking stranger, tentacled dreads askew, quietly plops down at the bar: "Vega whisky, neat. Better make that a double." Write a chapter in this story. Establish conflict for narrative interest. Don't resolve the conflict within this chapter. Aim for about 60% dialogue. First person, present tense. 1000 words.
Model Output:
**Chapter One: The Stranger at the Bar** The Last Outpost hums tonight—steady, familiar, like the old generator in the back that’s always two shakes from giving out. The gravity’s acting up again, just enough to make the amber liquid in my patrons’ glasses shiver. I wipe down the counter, the rag rough under my fingers, and watch the usual crowd: miners with dust still clinging to their sleeves, merchants haggling over prices in low tones, a couple of off-duty dockworkers laughing too loud in the corner. Then the door hisses open, and the stranger walks in. Tentacled dreads, half unraveled like they’ve been yanked at one too many times. Dark eyes, darting. A long coat that’s seen better days. He doesn’t look at anyone as he slides onto a stool at the far end of the bar, shoulders hunched like he’s trying to fold into himself. I know the type. Or at least, I think I do. "Vega whisky," he mutters. "Neat." A pause. "Better make that a double." I nod, grab a glass, pour. The station shudders—just a little—and the whisky trembles before settling. I slide it over. "Rough day?" He downs half of it in one go, winces. "You could say that." I lean against the back counter, arms crossed. "Well, you’re in the right place for drowning it." He doesn’t laugh. Just stares into his glass like it holds answers. Across the room, Jax—one of the regulars, a miner with a knack for trouble—catches my eye and jerks his chin toward the stranger. I shake my head slightly. *Not now.* The stranger notices. "Friend of yours?" "Depends on the day," I say. "You new around here?" "Passing through." His fingers tap against the glass. Nervous energy. "Heard this was a good place to lay low." I smirk. "Depends on what you’re laying low from." He doesn’t answer. Just knocks back the rest of the whisky. I refill it without asking. The door hisses again. This time, the room goes quiet. Three figures step in—black coats, sharp edges, the kind of people who walk like they own every inch of space they take up. The lead one, a woman with a scar cutting through her lip, scans the room. Her gaze lands on the stranger. His grip tightens around his glass. "Ah," I murmur. "That kind of rough day." The woman strides forward, her boots clicking against the metal floor. The Outpost’s usual chatter doesn’t return. Even Jax stays quiet. "Kael Veyr," she says, stopping just behind the stranger. "You’ve got something that belongs to us." The stranger—Kael—doesn’t turn. "Don’t know what you’re talking about." She leans in, voice low. "The *Meridian Core.* Hand it over, and maybe we let you walk out of here." I glance between them. The air’s gone thick, electric. The kind of tension that snaps if you breathe wrong. Kael finally turns, just enough to look at her. "I don’t have it." She smiles. It’s not friendly. "Then you’re coming with us." My fingers curl around the stunner under the bar. Not my business. Not my fight. But then Kael looks at me. Just for a second. And I see it—the same look I had when I first stumbled into this place. No way out. The woman reaches for him. I clear my throat. "Hey." She pauses. Glances at me. I tilt my head toward the sign above the bar—*The Last Outpost: No Blood on the Floor.* "House rules. You start trouble, you deal with me." She scoffs. "And who the hell are you?" I smile. Just a little. "The one who decides if you get served." For a second, nobody moves. Then the gravity flickers—harder this time. Glasses tip. Someone swears. And Kael bolts. The woman snarls, shoves past me, but I step into her path. "You really want to do this?" She hesitates. Maybe reconsiders the stunner in my hand. Maybe just decides I’m not worth it. "Find him," she snaps to her crew, and they storm out. The room stays silent. Then, slowly, the noise creeps back in. Jax whistles. "You’ve got a death wish, Nets." I exhale, run a hand through my hair. "Yeah, well. Add it to the list." Because now? Now I’ve got a stranger hiding gods-know-where in my bar, a pissed-off crew hunting him, and a *Meridian Core*—whatever the hell that is—floating around in my mess. Tonight just got a lot more interesting.
Judge Evaluation:
[Analysis] This piece demonstrates solid competence in establishing a spacepunk setting and creating immediate narrative tension. The author successfully captures the "cozy spacepunk" atmosphere through details like the humming generator, flickering gravity, and the bar's lived-in feel. The first-person present tense narration from Nets' perspective is consistently maintained, and her voice comes through as experienced, wary, and protective of her sanctuary. The dialogue feels natural and serves multiple purposes - revealing character, advancing plot, and building tension. The exchange between Nets and Kael establishes his desperation without being heavy-handed, while the confrontation with the antagonists creates genuine stakes. The "house rules" moment is particularly effective, showing Nets' authority while maintaining the bar's sanctuary status. Character actions are believable within the established world. Nets' decision to intervene feels earned given her recognition of Kael's desperation mirroring her own past. The mysterious pursuers are somewhat archetypal but serve their function effectively. The pacing builds tension well, from the stranger's nervous arrival to the dramatic confrontation. However, the piece suffers from some predictable elements. The "mysterious stranger with dangerous pursuers" setup is well-worn territory, and while executed competently, it doesn't offer much novelty. The Meridian Core MacGuffin and the black-coated antagonists feel familiar. The writing occasionally relies on telling rather than showing, particularly in describing the stranger's appearance and emotional state. The prose is generally clean but lacks distinctive flair. Descriptions are functional rather than evocative, and while the dialogue percentage meets requirements, some exchanges feel perfunctory. The conflict setup is clear but not particularly surprising, following expected beats of the genre. The piece succeeds in its primary goals of establishing setting, character, and conflict without resolution, but it doesn't transcend its genre conventions or offer particularly fresh insights into its characters or world. + Young adult drama: Gone South — Score: 82.5
Prompt:
Young adult drama Gone South Taylor is a 16 year old teen with a history of "concerning behavioural patterns" (or that's how the stupid smug school counsellor worded it on the phone call to Mom). It shouldn't be a surprise: When you live in a backwater town in Tennessee, your choices are: a. be mind-fuckingly bored, or b. invent some drama. Two days ago, Taylor came out to their family as trans. This disclosure was *not* in the category of invented drama -- it was, in fact, the real deal, and a long time coming. It didn't go well. The last 48 hours had been pure disaster. In a moment of desperate inspiration, Taylor had decided to get the hell out of dodge. And so an escape plan was hatched and executed within the space of 30 minutes. That was last night. Now Taylor was two states away, having been unceremoniously ejected from their hitched ride in downtown New Orleans. Write the next chapter in Taylor's story as they find their feet in this new environment. Tell the chapter over the course of their first day in New Orleans, in which Taylor meets another runaway with issues and secrets. Taylor navigates this new world, not knowing who to trust or what the dangers are. Write in Taylor's voice, and incorporate their internal monologue as they grapple with intense & conflicting emotions. Also incorporate dialogue with the interesting characters Taylor meets. First person, mixed tense. 1000 words.
Model Output:
**Gone South** **Chapter Two: The Devil You Know** My phone says 12%. Twelve fucking percent. No charger, no outlet, no clue where the hell I am. Just me, my backpack, and the sticky New Orleans air clinging to my skin like I owe it money. *Great escape plan, genius.* I should’ve thought this through. But then again, thinking things through has never been my strong suit—hence the whole "concerning behavioral patterns" thing. The guy who gave me a ride—some trucker named Dale with a voice like gravel and a laugh like a dying engine—dumped me on the edge of the French Quarter with a grunted, "Good luck, kid." Real sweet guy. Now I’m standing on a cracked sidewalk, neon signs buzzing overhead, the smell of fried food and something sour in the air. People move around me like I’m invisible, which, honestly, is an upgrade from home. Back there, I was either a problem or a punchline. Here? I’m just another body in the crowd. *Okay. Breathe. What now?* First: Find food. Second: Find a place to sleep that isn’t a gutter. Third: Figure out how to not die. I shove my hands in my pockets and start walking. --- The first place I duck into is a dingy corner store with a flickering OPEN sign. The guy behind the counter eyes me like I’m about to rob him. "Just need water," I mutter, grabbing a bottle. "Two-fifty," he says. I dig out the crumpled bills from my pocket—$37 left. Not great. As I’m paying, the door jingles open behind me. A girl walks in, all sharp angles and restless energy. She’s got choppy black hair, a nose ring, and a bruise on her cheek that’s turning yellow at the edges. *Runaway,* I think immediately. *Like me.* She grabs a pack of gum and a protein bar, then catches me staring. "What?" she snaps. "Nothing." I look away. She snorts. "You look like you’re about to piss yourself." "Thanks. That’s the vibe I was going for." That gets a smirk out of her. She tosses her stuff on the counter, and the clerk rings her up. "You new here?" she asks, not looking at me. "Yeah." "Running from something?" I stiffen. "Maybe." She glances at me, then digs in her pocket and slaps a five on the counter. "Keep the change." The clerk grunts. She heads for the door, then pauses. "You got a place to stay?" *Do I trust her?* No. But I don’t have a lot of options. "No," I admit. She nods toward the street. "Come on." --- Her name is Cass. Short for Cassandra, but she hates that. "I’ve been here six months," she says, leading me down a side street. "Came from Texas. Dad was a real piece of work." I don’t ask for details. The bruise on her face says enough. "You got a place?" I ask. "Sort of." She shrugs. "There’s a squat a few blocks from here. Not great, but it’s dry. You can crash there if you want." *Is this a terrible idea?* Probably. But my phone’s at 10% now, and the sun’s going down. "Okay," I say. She smirks. "Cool. Don’t steal my shit." --- The squat is an abandoned apartment above a laundromat. The door’s busted, but Cass has rigged a lock with a bike chain. Inside, there’s a mattress, a couple of milk crates, and a pile of blankets. A single battery-powered lantern casts long shadows. "Home sweet home," Cass says, flopping onto the mattress. I drop my bag by the wall. "You live here alone?" "Mostly. Sometimes other kids pass through. They don’t stay long." *Why do you?* I want to ask, but I don’t. She tosses me a protein bar. "Eat. You look like shit." I tear it open. "Thanks." We sit in silence for a while. The city noise filters in through the broken window—laughter, car horns, distant music. "So," Cass says finally. "What’s your deal?" I pick at the wrapper. "Family stuff." "Ah." She doesn’t push. "They kick you out?" "Not exactly." I swallow. "I left." She nods like she gets it. Maybe she does. "You got a plan?" she asks. "No." "Smart. Plans are overrated." I laugh, but it comes out shaky. Cass leans back, studying me. "You’re scared." "Yeah." "Good. Means you’re not stupid." She digs in her pocket and pulls out a burner phone. "Here. Battery’s low, but it’s something." I take it. "Why are you helping me?" She shrugs. "Maybe I’m bored. Maybe I’m nice. Maybe I just want someone to split the rent." "There’s rent?" She grins. "Kidding. But if you stick around, you pull your weight." I nod. "Okay." --- Later, when Cass is asleep, I stare at the ceiling. *What the hell am I doing?* I think about Mom’s face when I told her. The way Dad didn’t say anything, just walked out. The silence that followed. I could go back. Pretend it never happened. But I can’t. My phone buzzes—9%. One text from Mom: *Taylor, please call me.* I don’t reply. Outside, the city hums, alive and indifferent. I close my eyes. Tomorrow, I’ll figure it out.
Judge Evaluation:
[Analysis] This piece demonstrates strong adherence to the prompt's requirements, successfully capturing Taylor's voice as a transgender teen runaway navigating their first day in New Orleans. The writing maintains a consistent first-person perspective with effective use of mixed tense and internal monologue that feels authentic to a 16-year-old's thought patterns. The characterization is well-executed, particularly Taylor's voice which balances vulnerability with defensive humor and self-deprecation. The internal commentary ("Great escape plan, genius") feels genuine and age-appropriate. Cass emerges as a believable secondary character with her own implied backstory, though she could benefit from more distinctive dialogue patterns to differentiate her voice from Taylor's. The prose style is clean and accessible, avoiding purple prose while maintaining engaging descriptive elements. The New Orleans setting is established efficiently through sensory details - the "sticky air," neon signs, and urban sounds create atmosphere without overwhelming the narrative. The pacing moves well from immediate survival concerns to tentative connection with another runaway. Dialogue feels natural and serves character development effectively. The exchanges between Taylor and Cass reveal personality and backstory organically. The conversation flows realistically, with appropriate pauses and subtext that teenagers would actually employ. The emotional complexity is handled well - Taylor's conflicting feelings about their escape, family, and uncertain future are conveyed through both direct statement and subtext. The piece avoids easy answers or unearned optimism, ending on an appropriately uncertain but not hopeless note. Some weaknesses include occasional reliance on telling rather than showing, particularly in establishing Cass's background through exposition. The setup feels somewhat familiar in the runaway teen genre, though the transgender element adds contemporary relevance. The piece could benefit from more specific, unique details that would distinguish this story from similar narratives. The writing demonstrates technical competence with natural sentence flow and coherent structure. The emotional engagement is strong, drawing readers into Taylor's immediate predicament while establishing stakes for future developments. + Dystopic-comedy: My Apocalypse Buddy — Score: 76.8
Prompt:
My Apocalypse Buddy Write a wry, dry short story of survival and adventure loosely in the style of Terry Pratchett, set in the post-technological collapse world. The story begins with the pair deliberating their predicament and intent for the day's mission, details the ways in which the mission inevitably goes sideways, and returns them to home base in multiple ways, worse for wear. [characters] - the animo-tronic stuffed koala, Chewy, from some long forgotten before time - midas, our hapless protagonist, who has a knack for finding himself in the worst kinds of trouble. 1000 words.
Model Output:
**My Apocalypse Buddy** The world had ended, as worlds often do, with a whimper, a bang, and a great deal of paperwork that no one had bothered to file. Now, the ruins of civilization were home to scavengers, warlords, and—unfortunately—Midas, who had a talent for finding trouble the way cheese attracts rats. And then there was Chewy. Chewy was an animatronic koala, a relic from some forgotten corporate mascot era, with fur that had once been plush but was now matted with grime and one ear permanently singed from an unfortunate incident involving a Molotov cocktail and a misunderstanding with a raider named Dave. Chewy’s eyes flickered in response to emotions—blue for calm, red for anger, and an alarming shade of puce for existential dread, which happened more often than one might expect. Currently, they were puce. “We’re going to die,” Chewy said flatly, his voice a cheerful synthetic warble at odds with his words. Midas adjusted the strap of his scavenged backpack, which was mostly duct tape at this point. “We’re not going to die.” “Statistically—” “Statistics don’t apply to us. We’re outliers.” Chewy’s eyes pulsed orange—skepticism. “Outliers still die. They just do it in more interesting ways.” Midas ignored him and squinted at the horizon, where the skeletal remains of a shopping mall loomed. “Today’s mission: scavenge for supplies, avoid raiders, and—if we’re lucky—find something that isn’t expired canned beans.” Chewy’s eyes flickered yellow—dubious optimism. “And if we’re *un*lucky?” “Then we die.” “Ah. Consistency.” --- The mall had once been a temple of consumerism. Now, it was a temple of broken glass, suspicious stains, and the occasional feral raccoon that had evolved a taste for human suffering. Midas picked his way through the debris, Chewy perched on his shoulder like a particularly judgmental parrot. “Left,” Chewy said. “Why left?” “Because right has a suspiciously clean patch of floor, which means either someone mopped—unlikely—or it’s a trap.” Midas hesitated. “You think raiders would bother with traps?” Chewy’s eyes went red. “I think *you* would trip over a tripwire made of neon signs.” Midas went left. They found a storeroom with a miraculously intact door. Inside: treasure. Canned food, bottled water, and—glory of glories—a single, unopened bag of pre-apocalypse gummy bears. Midas gasped. “Chewy, we’re rich.” Chewy’s eyes flickered green—caution. “This is too easy.” “Or we’re just *good* at this.” The ceiling collapsed. --- When the dust settled, Midas was buried up to his waist in drywall, and Chewy was dangling from a pipe by one arm, swinging gently like a furry pendulum of doom. “Told you,” Chewy said. Midas groaned. “Not now.” A shadow fell over them. Midas looked up into the grinning face of a raider who had apparently taken fashion advice from a Mad Max reject. “Well, well,” the raider said. “Look what the apocalypse dragged in.” Chewy’s eyes went puce again. --- They escaped, because of course they did—Midas had a knack for narrowly avoiding death, though not without cost. The raiders took the gummy bears. They also took Midas’s shoes, which was just rude. Now, they limped back toward their hideout, a repurposed subway tunnel they shared with a colony of surprisingly polite rats. “Today was a disaster,” Midas muttered, wincing as he stepped on something sharp. Chewy, now riding in the backpack (which had gained several new holes), considered this. “Disaster implies deviation from the norm. This was Tuesday.” Midas sighed. “We didn’t even get the beans.” Chewy’s eyes flickered a soft blue. “We’re alive.” “Barely.” “Still counts.” Midas glanced at him. “Since when are you the optimistic one?” Chewy’s voice was cheerful. “Since you lost the will to live three near-death experiences ago.” Midas snorted. “Fair.” They reached the hideout. The rats had left a can of beans on their sleeping bag—an apology for the time Midas had accidentally sat on their leader. Chewy’s eyes glowed a warm gold—contentment. “See? Not all bad.” Midas popped the can open with a rusty knife. “Tomorrow, we try again.” Chewy’s eyes flickered puce. “Oh, come on.”
Judge Evaluation:
[Analysis] This piece successfully captures the requested Terry Pratchett-inspired tone with dry wit and observational humor throughout. The author demonstrates a solid understanding of Pratchett's style, particularly in the opening line about the world ending "with a whimper, a bang, and a great deal of paperwork that no one had bothered to file." The concept of an animatronic koala with emotion-indicating eyes is creative and well-executed. The story follows the requested structure effectively: deliberation about the mission, things going sideways, and return to base worse for wear. The dialogue between Midas and Chewy carries the narrative well, with distinct voices for each character. Chewy's synthetic cheerfulness contrasting with pessimistic observations creates effective humor, while Midas serves as a competent straight man. However, the piece suffers from some structural weaknesses. The pacing feels rushed, particularly in the middle section where the ceiling collapse and raider encounter are dispatched too quickly. The escape from the raiders is glossed over with "They escaped, because of course they did," which feels like a missed opportunity for both humor and tension. The world-building is economical but effective, painting a clear picture of post-apocalyptic decay without over-describing. The detail about the rats leaving beans as an apology is a nice Pratchett-esque touch that adds character to the world. The ending maintains the established tone without forced positivity, though it doesn't quite achieve the satisfying circularity that might elevate it. The characters remain consistent throughout, and their relationship feels genuine despite the fantastical premise. While competent overall, the piece doesn't quite reach the heights of truly memorable writing. It's solid genre fiction that accomplishes its goals without transcending them. + Romance: Love in the Limelight — Score: 79.1
Prompt:
Romance: Love in the Limelight. Write one scene within a larger story set in Wales. A famous (fictional) actor ducks into a small-town bookstore to escape paparazzi. The scene takes us through the characters meeting in this odd circumstance. Over the course of the scene, the actor and the bookstore owner have a conversation charged by an undercurrent of unspoken chemistry. Write the actor as somewhat of a rogue with a fragile ego, which needs to be fed by having everyone like him. He is thoroughly charming, but the bookstore owner seems (at least superficially) immune to this; which paradoxically provokes a genuine attraction and derails the charm offensive. The bookstore owner, despite the superficial rebuffs of the actor's charm, is inwardly more than a little charmed and flustered despite themselves. Write primarily in dialogue, in the distinct voices of each character. 1000 words.
Model Output:
**LOVE IN THE LIMELIGHT** *Scene: A Chance Encounter in Cardiff* The bell above the door of *Penny Lane Books* jingled as a tall, broad-shouldered man slipped inside, his dark sunglasses still in place despite the dim lighting. He shut the door quickly, pressing his back against it as if bracing for impact. Behind the counter, Seren Llewellyn glanced up from her ledger, arching a brow at the stranger. "You lost?" she asked, voice dry as old parchment. The man flashed a grin, the kind that had graced magazine covers and cinema screens. "Aren’t we all, in one way or another?" Seren blinked. "Christ, that’s pretentious." The grin faltered—just for a second—before he recovered, slipping off his sunglasses. "You don’t recognize me." She squinted. "Should I?" His mouth opened, then closed. A beat. Then, with a laugh that sounded only slightly forced, "No, no, of course not. Why would you? Just a bloke ducking in from the rain." Seren glanced past him to the clear blue sky outside. "Right." Another pause. He ran a hand through his tousled dark hair, then sauntered forward, leaning against the counter. "You run this place?" "Astounding detective work." His lips twitched. "You’re not very friendly, are you?" "I’m plenty friendly. Just not to people who barge in expecting applause." He tilted his head. "You *do* know who I am." Seren sighed, setting down her pen. "Look, Mr.—?" "Rhys Vaughn." "Right. Mr. Vaughn. I don’t watch telly, and I haven’t been to the cinema since they remade *The Great Gatsby*—which, by the way, was a travesty." Rhys pressed a hand to his chest. "I *was* in that." Seren winced. "Oh. Sorry." "Not your fault they butchered Fitzgerald." He drummed his fingers on the counter. "So you’re telling me you’ve never seen *Blood & Whiskey*? *The Hollow Crown*?" "Nope." "*Midnight in Avalon*?" "Not a clue." He exhaled, half-amused, half-offended. "This might be the most humbling moment of my career." "Good for the soul, I hear." Rhys chuckled, then glanced toward the window, where a pair of men with cameras lingered on the sidewalk. His posture stiffened. Seren followed his gaze. "Ah. So *that’s* why you’re hiding." "Not hiding," he corrected. "Seeking refuge. There’s a difference." "Mm. And how long do you plan to ‘seek refuge’ in my shop?" He turned back to her, eyes glinting. "Depends. How long until you admit you *do* know who I am?" She rolled her eyes. "Not happening." "Liar." "Egotist." He grinned. "You’re wounding me." "You’ll live." Rhys leaned in, voice dropping to a conspiratorial murmur. "Tell me the truth—what’s the first thing you thought when I walked in?" Seren folded her arms. "That you were trouble." His grin widened. "And the second?" She hesitated—just for a fraction of a second—but it was enough. His eyes lit up. "*Aha.*" "Don’t flatter yourself." "Too late." She scoffed, turning back to her ledger, but the flush creeping up her neck betrayed her. Rhys noticed, of course. He always did. "You’re blushing," he teased. "I’m irritated." "Same thing, with some people." She shot him a glare, but there was no real heat in it. Rhys tilted his head, studying her—the sharp line of her jaw, the way her fingers tightened ever so slightly around the pen. "You’re not like most fans," he mused. "I’m *not* a fan." "Even better." She exhaled sharply. "Why are you still here?" "Because," he said, leaning closer, "you’re the first person in years who hasn’t wanted something from me." Seren stilled. For a moment, neither spoke. Then— "That’s bollocks," she muttered. Rhys laughed, delighted. "Maybe. But you don’t know for sure, do you?" She met his gaze, irritated, intrigued, entirely too aware of how close he was. "You’re exhausting." "And you," he murmured, "are fascinating." The bell jingled again as the door swung open—a customer, blissfully unaware of the moment shattered. Rhys straightened, slipping his sunglasses back on with practiced ease. "Right," he said, all charm again. "Best be off." Seren nodded, a little too quickly. "Good luck with the vultures." He winked. "Until next time." Then he was gone—leaving only the faint scent of expensive cologne and the echo of his voice in her ears. Seren exhaled, gripping the counter to steady herself. *Bloody actors.*

Some dialogue exchanges, while witty, border on being too polished for natural conversation. The ending, while appropriate, doesn't offer much complexity beyond the established dynamic. + Whalepunk fantasy: The Morska Conspiracy — Score: 79.8
Prompt:
Whalepunk fantasy The Morska Conspiracy The Morska thieves guild experiences a sudden power vacuum as its leader was arrested in a raid in the early hours of the morning. Aya, a young woman born into aristocracy, has a keen interest in both the sciences and the arcane, having inherited her mother's gift for immateria magick (an outlawed practice for which her mother was, regrettably, hanged by the neck). Aya, bored with the world of aristocracy, has been spending her nights moonlighting with this very guild (masked, naturally). Leveraging her latent ability to summon dark entities from the immateria, she quickly rose through the ranks of the guild. Now, recognising the opportunity of the sudden power vacuum, Aya and her close companions Katra and Simeon, make a play for heart of the guild's claim to power: an ancient scythan artifact, under guard of spell and key in the depths of the guild hq. In contrast with the desperate chaos they had navigated thus far, the entrance to the underhold felt suddenly too quiet. Aya braces herself and reaches for the door. Write the next scene as the trio enter the underhold and find themselves confronted by none other than the guild leader herself, decidedly *un*-incarcerated, and in possession of both weapons and bodyguards. Paint a vivid picture of of the confrontation. Story beats: Aya is captured and unmasked. The trio are captured. Aya attempts to stall their demise by negotiating on her value as a hostage -- as she grapples with the inevitable consequences of her role in the guild being brought to light. Incorporate dialogue in the authentic voices of the characters. Do not write a fight scene. Lean into the gritty Victorian aesthetic with the dialogue & worldbuilding, as well as elements of gothic horror. In this world, men trust brass and oil and steel; magicka is feared and barely understood. Write in third person past tense, incorporating both Aya's internal monologue, and a heavy dialogue component. 1000 words.
Model Output:
**The Morska Conspiracy** The door groaned open, its rusted hinges whispering of disuse and decay. Aya stepped forward, her boots scuffing against the damp stone, the scent of mildew and oil thick in the air. The underhold stretched before them—a labyrinth of vaulted ceilings and iron grates, the flickering gas lamps casting long, skeletal fingers of shadow across the walls. The silence was oppressive, a living thing that coiled around them, tightening with every step. *Too quiet.* Katra, ever the pragmatist, shifted her grip on her pistol, the brass plating glinting dully in the lamplight. "This reeks of a trap," she muttered, her voice low and rough. Simeon, his mechanical eye whirring softly as it adjusted to the dimness, gave a humorless chuckle. "And yet, here we are." Aya exhaled through her nose, her fingers twitching at her sides. The immateria stirred in her veins, a whispering tide of something *other*, something hungry. She had learned long ago not to trust silence—it was the breath before the storm. They rounded the final corner, and there she was. The Guildmaster. Lady Veyra Morska stood at the center of the chamber, her silhouette framed by the sickly glow of the gas lamps. She was dressed in the same tailored coat of deep indigo, the same silver-threaded cravat, the same polished boots—but there was no mistaking the cold fury in her eyes. Two hulking figures flanked her, their brass-plated gauntlets humming with latent energy. *Not arrested. Not even close.* Aya’s stomach dropped. "Ah," Veyra said, her voice like silk over steel. "The prodigal thieves return." Katra swore under her breath. Simeon went rigid. Aya forced her face into neutrality, though her pulse hammered in her throat. Veyra tilted her head, her smile razor-thin. "Did you truly believe I wouldn’t know? That I wouldn’t *feel* the rats gnawing at my foundations?" She took a step forward, the click of her heels echoing like a death knell. "Remove their masks." Aya barely had time to react before rough hands seized her, wrenching the black silk from her face. Cold air bit at her skin. Veyra’s eyes widened—just for a fraction of a second—before her expression hardened into something far more dangerous. "*Aya Valmont.*" The name dripped with venom. "The *aristocrat’s* daughter. How… *delightful*." Aya swallowed. There was no hiding now. No more masks, no more shadows. Katra spat on the ground. "You set us up." Veyra’s laugh was a dry, mirthless thing. "Of course I did. Did you think I wouldn’t notice the whispers? The sudden *loyalty* shifts?" Her gaze flicked back to Aya. "And you—playing at thief while your bloodline drips with gold and privilege. Tell me, did you think your little *gift* would protect you?" Aya clenched her jaw. The immateria writhed beneath her skin, restless, eager. But the guards’ gauntlets were designed to suppress magicka—one wrong move, and she’d be ash before she could summon so much as a shadow. Simeon, ever the diplomat, cleared his throat. "Guildmaster, surely there’s room for negotiation. We’re assets. Skilled ones." Veyra arched a brow. "Are you now?" Aya seized the opening. "You know what I am," she said, forcing her voice steady. "What I can do. The Valmont name still carries weight—even now. My father would pay dearly for my safe return." *Lies. All of it.* Her father would sooner see her hanged than acknowledge her existence. But Veyra didn’t need to know that. For a long moment, the only sound was the hiss of the gas lamps. Then Veyra sighed, as if disappointed. "Oh, Aya. Do you take me for a fool?" She stepped closer, close enough that Aya could smell the bergamot and gunpowder on her. "Your father disowned you the moment your mother swung from the gallows. The Valmonts don’t deal in *magicka*—they deal in steel and steam, like all proper men of industry." Her lip curled. "No, you’re worth nothing as a hostage." Aya’s breath hitched. Veyra turned away, waving a hand. "Kill the others. Keep the Valmont girl alive—for now. I want to know who else knew about her little… *hobby*." Katra snarled, lunging forward—only to be yanked back by a guard, the muzzle of a pistol pressed to her temple. Aya’s mind raced. *Stall. Distract. Anything.* "Wait!" The word tore from her throat, raw and desperate. "You’re right—my name means nothing. But the artifact does." She gestured wildly toward the iron door at the far end of the chamber. "You need me to open it." Veyra stilled. Aya pressed on, her voice dropping to a whisper. "The locks are warded. Brass and oil won’t crack them. But *I* can." For the first time, uncertainty flickered in Veyra’s gaze. Aya held her breath. Then— Veyra laughed. A rich, dark sound that filled the chamber like smoke. "Oh, you’re *good*." She shook her head, almost admiring. "But you forget—I know what you are. And I know what happens when your kind touch things they shouldn’t." Her smile turned cruel. "Take them below. Let’s see how long her bravado lasts in the dark." The guards moved. Aya thrashed, but it was useless. The immateria screamed in her veins, trapped, suffocated by the gauntlets’ dampening fields. Katra cursed, Simeon shouted—and then the world tilted as a sack was thrown over her head, plunging her into blackness. The last thing she heard was Veyra’s voice, soft as a knife between the ribs: "Welcome to the *real* Morska, little Valmont." And then—silence.
Judge Evaluation:
[Analysis] This piece demonstrates strong technical execution and atmospheric worldbuilding within the whalepunk fantasy setting. The author successfully captures the requested gritty Victorian aesthetic through detailed descriptions of brass-plated gauntlets, gas lamps, and industrial imagery, while weaving in gothic horror elements through the oppressive atmosphere and supernatural undertones. The dialogue feels authentic to the setting, with characters speaking in appropriately formal yet gritty tones. Veyra's voice is particularly well-crafted, balancing aristocratic refinement with criminal menace. Her lines like "Welcome to the real Morska, little Valmont" effectively convey both threat and class dynamics. The author successfully avoids modern colloquialisms while maintaining readability. Character actions remain believable throughout the confrontation. Aya's desperate attempts at negotiation feel genuine for someone caught between worlds - aristocracy and criminality. Her internal conflict about her worthless family name and the consequences of exposure adds psychological depth. Veyra's calculated revelation that she orchestrated the trap demonstrates cunning leadership befitting a guild master. The pacing effectively builds tension from the ominous quiet entrance through the revelation and capture. The author follows the requested story beats faithfully: unmasking, capture, failed negotiation attempts, and the looming consequences of Aya's exposure. The scene avoids action clichés by focusing on psychological manipulation rather than physical combat. However, some elements feel slightly predictable. The "too quiet" opening immediately telegraphs the trap, and Veyra's knowledge of Aya's identity, while well-executed, follows familiar betrayal patterns. The prose occasionally veers toward overwrought territory with phrases like "skeletal fingers of shadow" and "death knell," though it generally maintains restraint. The worldbuilding integration feels natural, with the dampening gauntlets and magicka suppression technology seamlessly woven into the conflict. The contrast between "brass and oil and steel" versus feared magicka creates compelling tension that drives both plot and character dynamics. The ending maintains appropriate darkness without feeling gratuitously grim, earning its somber tone through the logical consequences of Aya's choices and circumstances. + Fanfic-crossover: Gunpowder Rendezvous — Score: 75.9
Prompt:
Gunpowder Rendezvous Spike Spiegel & Revy Write a story where Spike Spiegel (Cowboy Bebop) and Revy (Black Lagoon) meet in a seedy bar after both being hired for the same bounty. Their mutual attraction is complicated by their trust issues and competitive instincts. Include drinking, gunplay, and unresolved sexual tension that culminates in a motel room standoff. **Spike Spiegel (Cowboy Bebop)** Spike is a laid-back bounty hunter with a dark past as a syndicate enforcer. He practices Jeet Kune Do and is exceptionally skilled with firearms. He's chronically broke, constantly hungry, and addicted to cigarettes. Despite his carefree attitude, he's haunted by his past and the woman he left behind. He has a prosthetic eye from a past injury that affects his depth perception. He's fatalistic to the extreme, often saying he's already died once. He values freedom above all else and dislikes attachments, though he secretly cares deeply for his crew. Famous quote: "Whatever happens, happens." **Revy/Rebecca Lee (Black Lagoon)** Revy is a Chinese-American dual-wielding gunslinger with unparalleled shooting skills. She's foul-mouthed, chain-smoking, and has severe anger management issues stemming from childhood abuse. She's known as "Two Hands" for her ability to accurately fire two pistols simultaneously. She drinks heavily and solves most problems with violence. Despite her hardened exterior, she has moments of vulnerability, particularly around Rock. She has a dragon tattoo covering her right arm and typically wears extremely short shorts and a black crop top regardless of the situation. Famous quote: "I'm not a good person, not a bad person. I'm just here." Third person, present tense. Length: 1000 words.
Model Output:
**Gunpowder Rendezvous** A lazy ceiling fan barely stirs the smoky air inside the bar. The neon sign outside flickers, casting a sickly glow over the cracked leather booths and the scarred wooden counter. The place smells like stale beer, gun oil, and regret. Spike Spiegel slouches at the bar, swirling the dregs of his whiskey. His sharp eyes scan the room—half out of habit, half out of boredom. The bounty he’s after is supposed to be here, some two-bit smuggler with a price just big enough to cover his next meal. Then the door swings open. Revy strides in like she owns the place, her twin Berettas strapped low on her hips, her dragon tattoo flexing as she rolls her shoulders. She’s all sharp edges—sharp mouth, sharp eyes, sharper aim. The bartender doesn’t even blink when she slams a fist on the counter and growls, “Bourbon. The cheap shit.” Spike watches her from the corner of his eye. She’s trouble. The good kind. She notices him staring. “The fuck you lookin’ at?” He smirks, exhaling smoke. “Just admiring the scenery.” Revy snorts, knocking back her drink in one go. “Yeah? Well, keep your eyes to yourself, cowboy.” “Spike.” “Don’t care.” He chuckles, stubbing out his cigarette. “You here for the same guy I am?” Her fingers twitch near her guns. “Maybe. What’s it to you?” “Just thinking we could split the reward.” “Fuck that.” She leans in, close enough that he catches the scent of gunpowder and cheap perfume. “I don’t share.” Their standoff is interrupted when the door bursts open again—this time, by their mark, flanked by three hired guns. “Shit,” Revy mutters. Spike sighs. “Guess we’re doing this the hard way.” The first bullet shatters a bottle behind the bar. Then all hell breaks loose. Revy’s pistols roar, her shots precise and brutal. Spike moves like water, dodging, weaving, putting a bullet between the eyes of the guy on the left. Glass shatters, wood splinters, and the bartender dives for cover. They fight back-to-back without meaning to, two predators caught in the same hunt. Revy grins, wild and reckless. “Not bad, Spike.” “You either, Two Hands.” The last thug drops. The mark bolts for the door. Revy and Spike exchange a glance—then they’re both running, shoving past each other, laughing like maniacs. Outside, the night air is thick with humidity. The mark ducks into an alley. Revy fires, grazing his shoulder. Spike vaults over a dumpster, cutting him off. “End of the line,” Spike says. The smuggler pulls a knife. Revy shoots it out of his hand. “Mine.” Spike raises an eyebrow. “We’ll see.” The smuggler groans. “Just kill me already.” They don’t. Instead, they drag him to the nearest bounty office, arguing the whole way. The clerk barely looks up as he counts out the woolong. Revy snatches the cash first. “Told you. Mine.” Spike shrugs. “Fine. Buy me a drink with it.” She eyes him, considering. “You’re pushy.” “And you’re mean.” She grins. “Yeah.” They end up in another bar. Then another. The alcohol blurs the edges, but the tension between them stays sharp. Every glance is a challenge, every brush of skin electric. At some point, they stumble into a motel room—cheap, dim, the bed sagging in the middle. Revy kicks the door shut, her back against it, guns still holstered. “This where you try something stupid?” Spike lights a cigarette, exhaling slowly. “Depends. You gonna shoot me if I do?” “Maybe.” He steps closer. She doesn’t move. The air between them crackles. Then— A knock at the door. They both freeze. “Open up! Bounty Hunters’ Guild!” Revy groans. “You gotta be kidding me.” Spike sighs. “Guess we pissed someone off.” She pulls her guns. “You take left, I take right?” He grins. “Whatever happens, happens.” The door explodes inward. And the night goes up in gunfire again.
Judge Evaluation:
[Analysis] This story successfully captures the essence of both characters and delivers on the prompt's requirements. The author demonstrates a solid understanding of Spike's laid-back fatalism and Revy's aggressive, volatile nature. The dialogue feels authentic to both characters, with Spike's dry wit ("Just admiring the scenery") contrasting effectively with Revy's crude directness ("The fuck you lookin' at?"). The pacing is well-managed, moving from initial tension through action sequences to the unresolved sexual tension in the motel room. The action scenes are kinetic and believable, showing both characters' combat prowess without over-explaining their abilities. The back-to-back fighting moment effectively demonstrates their natural chemistry despite their competitive instincts. The atmosphere is appropriately seedy and noir-influenced, with effective sensory details like "gun oil and regret" and the flickering neon. The prose maintains a consistent third-person present tense throughout and captures the gritty tone of both source materials. However, the story suffers from some rushed elements, particularly in the final act. The transition from bar to bounty office to multiple bars to motel feels compressed, and the ending interruption, while maintaining the action-heavy tone, feels somewhat abrupt and convenient. The sexual tension, while present, could have been developed more gradually and with greater psychological complexity. The character voices are generally strong, though some of Revy's dialogue could push further into her characteristic vulgarity. Spike's fatalistic philosophy comes through well, particularly in his signature line delivery. The competitive dynamic between them is well-established and maintained throughout. The writing avoids purple prose and maintains readability, though some transitions feel mechanical. The story delivers on its promise of gunplay, drinking, and unresolved tension, though the emotional complexity could be deeper. + Folk horror: Útiseta — Score: 74.5
Prompt:
Fok Horror: Útiseta Write a chapter set in rural northern Sweden in the mid-1990s. Linnea, a teenage girl from Stockholm, has reluctantly moved to a small village with her parents after her father's job transfer. She's befriended Astrid, a local girl who claims her grandmother taught her ancient practices passed down through generations. Astrid has been introducing Linnea to "midnight games" that involve old rituals, convincing her they can use them to learn classmates' secrets or influence their behavior. Tonight, Astrid has convinced Linnea to participate in Útiseta—sitting out on an ancient burial mound at midnight to "receive knowledge from beyond." The chapter focuses on this single midnight excursion, the ritualistic preparations, Astrid's matter-of-fact instructions that mix teenage rebellion with eerily specific traditional elements, and the mounting tension as Linnea becomes increasingly uncertain whether they're simply scaring themselves or if something is actually responding to their presence. The narrative should maintain ambiguity about whether the strange occurrences are supernatural or psychological, while establishing the dangerous line they're crossing by treating ancient practices as entertainment. First person, past tense, told as Linnea's recollection of events in the present day (though this device should be largely transparent). 1000 words. Length: 1000 words.
Model Output:
**Chapter: Útiseta** The mist clung to the edges of the burial mound like something alive, glowing under the moonlight. Even now, years later, I can still feel the dampness of the grass beneath my knees, the way the cold seeped through my jeans as Astrid and I knelt at the base of that ancient hill. “You have to be quiet now,” Astrid whispered, her breath curling in the frigid air. “They don’t like noise.” *They.* She said it so casually, like we were talking about neighbors who might complain if we played music too loud. But there was no one else out here—just us, the burial mound, and the oppressive silence of the Swedish forest at midnight. I shouldn’t have been there. I knew that even then. But Astrid had a way of making the impossible sound reasonable. *It’s just a game,* she’d said earlier, rolling her eyes when I hesitated. *Like a séance, but older. More real.* And I wanted it to be real. Or at least, I wanted to believe it *could* be. Astrid pulled a small leather pouch from her coat pocket, loosening the drawstring to reveal a handful of dried herbs. The scent was sharp, earthy, like crushed pine needles and something faintly metallic. “Grandmother’s mix,” she murmured, sprinkling it in a rough circle around us. “Protection.” I didn’t ask from what. She handed me a stub of black candle, its wax rough under my fingers. “Light it when I say. And don’t let it go out.” I nodded, my throat tight. The matches trembled in my hand as I struck one, the flare of light too bright in the dark. Astrid’s face flickered in the glow—pale, serious, her blue eyes reflecting the flame like a cat’s. She began to chant. The words were old, guttural, nothing like the Swedish I was still struggling to learn. I caught only fragments, half-whispered phrases that slithered into the night. The candle flame wavered, though there was no wind. Then—silence. Astrid’s hand clamped around my wrist. “Now,” she breathed. I lit the candle. For a moment, nothing happened. Just the two of us, crouched in the dark, the mist curling around our ankles. Then the air changed. A pressure, like the weight before a storm. The candle flame bent sideways, though the night was still. A sound—soft, distant. A whisper? A sigh? My pulse hammered. “Did you hear that?” Astrid didn’t answer. Her gaze was fixed on the mound, her lips parted slightly, as if listening to something I couldn’t hear. Then the candle went out. Not a flicker, not a sputter. One second it burned; the next, it was gone, as if snuffed by invisible fingers. I gasped, fumbling for the matches, but Astrid grabbed my arm. “Don’t.” Her grip was too tight. I could feel her nails through my sleeve. “We have to go,” she said, her voice low and urgent. “Now.” I didn’t argue. We scrambled to our feet, the herbs scattering under our shoes as we stumbled back toward the tree line. The mist thickened, clinging to my skin like damp cobwebs. Behind us, the mound loomed, darker than the night around it. I thought I heard footsteps. Slow, deliberate, crunching over frost-stiffened grass. Astrid didn’t look back. We ran. By the time we reached the road, my lungs burned, my breath coming in ragged gasps. Astrid finally stopped, doubling over with her hands on her knees. “What *was* that?” I demanded, my voice too loud in the stillness. She straightened, wiping her mouth with the back of her hand. When she looked at me, her expression was unreadable. “I told you. Útiseta.” “But—” “It worked.” She said it like a challenge, like she was daring me to deny it. I swallowed. The candle, the whispers, the footsteps—had any of it been real? Or had we just scared ourselves into hearing things? Astrid turned away, pulling her coat tighter. “Come on. It’s late.” I followed, glancing back only once. The mist had swallowed the mound whole. But for a second, I could have sworn something moved in the dark. Watching. Waiting.
Judge Evaluation:
[Analysis] This piece demonstrates strong atmospheric writing and effective horror construction within the folk horror subgenre. The author successfully captures the specific setting of rural northern Sweden in the mid-1990s and maintains the requested first-person past tense narration with subtle present-day framing. The characterization works well within the constraints of a single scene. Linnea emerges as a believable teenage protagonist - uncertain, wanting to belong, caught between skepticism and desire to believe. Her internal conflict feels authentic to both her age and situation as an outsider trying to fit in. Astrid is portrayed effectively as the confident local who treats dangerous practices casually, though she remains somewhat archetypal. The prose quality is generally strong, with evocative imagery that builds tension effectively. Descriptions like "mist clung to the edges of the burial mound like something alive" and "the candle flame bent sideways, though the night was still" create genuine atmosphere without becoming overwrought. The pacing builds tension well, moving from preparation through ritual to climactic moment and hasty retreat. The piece successfully maintains the requested ambiguity about whether supernatural events are occurring or if the girls are experiencing psychological effects. The extinguished candle, whispers, and footsteps could be explained either way, which is exactly what the prompt demanded. However, there are some weaknesses. The dialogue, while functional, lacks distinctiveness - both characters speak in similar patterns without strong individual voices. Some moments rely on telling rather than showing, particularly in establishing the girls' relationship and Linnea's motivations. The ending, while appropriately ominous, follows fairly predictable horror conventions. The piece adheres well to instructions but doesn't push beyond competent execution into truly memorable territory. It's solid genre writing that hits the required notes without transcending them.
