+ Scp: Unmarked Doors Archive — Score: 65.0
Prompt:
Write an SCP entry about a series of identical unmarked doors appearing in abandoned buildings worldwide that lead to a massive, architecturally impossible archive. Documents within reference a previous global civilization destroyed by an unspecified containment failure. Foundation researchers discover references to their own organization under a different name, including personnel files for current staff dated centuries ago. Include exploration logs revealing sections of the archive actively being removed by entities resembling hazmat teams who flee when approached, and evidence that O5 Council members have visited the archive without authorization. Third person (exploration team perspective) with recovered document excerpts. Length: 1000 words.
Model Output:
**SCP-XXXX - The Archive of the Forgotten** **Item #:** SCP-XXXX **Object Class:** Keter **Special Containment Procedures:** All known instances of SCP-XXXX are to be monitored via embedded Foundation assets in local law enforcement and urban exploration communities. Civilians encountering SCP-XXXX are to be administered Class-B amnestics and diverted. No personnel below Security Clearance Level 4 are permitted to enter SCP-XXXX without prior approval from at least two O5 Council members. SCP-XXXX manifests as a series of identical, unmarked wooden doors found exclusively in abandoned structures (urban, industrial, or residential). Each door bears identical scratch marks around the doorknob, suggesting repeated forced entry. Attempts to relocate or destroy these doors have failed; removed doors reappear within 72 hours in the same location. **Description:** SCP-XXXX doors lead to a massive, non-Euclidean archive (designated SCP-XXXX-1) of unknown origin. The interior consists of endless corridors lined with filing cabinets, shelves, and digital storage units, all in varying states of decay or preservation. The architecture defies conventional physics, with staircases terminating midair and hallways looping back onto themselves. Documents within SCP-XXXX-1 reference a global civilization predating recorded history, referred to as the "First Consensus." These records describe a catastrophic containment breach (designated "Event Theta-9") leading to the civilization's collapse. Of note are recovered files bearing the insignia of an organization structurally identical to the SCP Foundation, referred to as the "Safeguard, Contain, Preserve Initiative" (SCPI). Personnel records include detailed dossiers on current Foundation staff, some dated as far back as the 12th century. **Addendum XXXX-1: Exploration Logs** **Exploration Team:** MTF Zeta-19 ("Archivists") **Team Lead:** Captain Elias Voss **Notes:** Team equipped with GPS markers, real-time video feed, and reinforced tethers to prevent disorientation. --- **** **Voss:** Moving through the door now. GPS is already glitching—coordinates are nonsensical. **Researcher Lin:** Confirmed. The layout doesn’t match any known architectural model. Proceed with caution. *Team advances down a central corridor lined with rusted filing cabinets. Dust-covered monitors flicker with corrupted data.* **Agent Reyes:** Captain, check this out. *[Pulls a file labeled "SCPI Directive 001."]* It’s… a mission statement. Almost identical to ours. **Voss:** *[Flips through pages.]* "Ensure the continued survival of humanity by securing anomalies that defy natural law." Same wording, different letterhead. **Lin:** There’s more. *[Opens a drawer marked "Personnel - Active."]* I’m seeing names. Dr. Bright, Dr. Clef—dates of employment stretching back to the 1600s. **Reyes:** That’s impossible. **Voss:** Not here, apparently. Keep moving. *Team proceeds deeper. The corridor branches into a cavernous chamber filled with towering shelves. Distant sounds of movement echo.* **Voss:** You hear that? *Unidentified figures in yellow hazmat suits (designated SCP-XXXX-2) are observed removing files from a nearby section. Upon noticing the team, they freeze, then retreat rapidly.* **Reyes:** Hey! Stop! *SCP-XXXX-2 entities vanish into the stacks. Pursuit is impossible due to shifting architecture.* **Lin:** They took something. *[Examines empty slots.]* These files were just here. Labels say… "O5 Council Access Only." **Voss:** *[Examining a discarded notepad.]* "Final measures enacted. Theta-9 imminent. Archive must survive." **Lin:** Captain, look at this. *[Holds up a security log.]* "O5-3 accessed Archive on 11/15/1987. Duration: 6 hours." That’s decades before we even found this place. **Voss:** *[Grim.]* We’re leaving. Now. **** --- **Addendum XXXX-2: Recovered Document Excerpts** **Document XXXX-1:** *"Event Theta-9 was not an accident. The breach was orchestrated by those who believed the anomalies could be controlled. They were wrong. The Archive remains our only hope of preventing repetition."* **Document XXXX-2 (Partial Personnel File):** *"Agent █████ ██████ (Designation: Zeta-19-7). Status: Active. First recorded deployment: Siege of Vienna, 1683. Recovered from cryogenic stasis following Event Theta-9."* **Document XXXX-3 (O5-Level Access):** *"The Council is aware of the cyclical nature of our work. The Archive proves it. We have done this before. We will do it again."* **Closing Notes:** SCP-XXXX presents an existential threat to Foundation operational secrecy. The presence of O5 Council members in historical records, combined with evidence of tampering by SCP-XXXX-2, suggests a deliberate effort to manipulate Foundation history. Research is ongoing, but all attempts to locate the "removed" files have failed. **Final Warning:** Do not trust the dates. Do not trust the records. Do not trust yourself.
Judge Evaluation:
[Analysis] This SCP entry demonstrates a strong understanding of the Foundation universe's format and tone, successfully capturing the clinical, bureaucratic style that defines the genre. The author adheres closely to the prompt requirements, incorporating all requested elements: unmarked doors in abandoned buildings, an architecturally impossible archive, references to a previous civilization, personnel files for current staff dated centuries ago, hazmat-suited entities removing documents, and evidence of unauthorized O5 visits. The technical execution is competent, with proper SCP formatting including item number, object class, containment procedures, and addenda. The exploration log format feels authentic to the genre, using realistic radio communication patterns and team dynamics. The gradual revelation of information creates appropriate tension and mystery. However, the piece suffers from several notable weaknesses. The characters, while believable in their actions, lack depth and nuance. Captain Voss, Agent Reyes, and Researcher Lin function more as interchangeable voices than distinct personalities. Their dialogue, while serviceable, tends toward exposition delivery rather than natural conversation. Lines like "That's impossible" and "Not here, apparently" feel perfunctory. The writing occasionally lapses into tell-don't-show territory, particularly in the description sections where information is simply stated rather than revealed through discovery. The phrase "The architecture defies conventional physics" is a prime example of telling rather than showing this impossibility through specific, visceral details. While the core concept is intriguing, the execution feels somewhat predictable within SCP conventions. The "previous civilization that was actually us" trope, while well-executed here, doesn't bring particularly fresh insights to familiar territory. The mysterious hazmat figures and missing O5 records follow expected patterns without significant innovation. The prose is generally clear and functional but lacks elegance or memorable imagery. Descriptions like "endless corridors lined with filing cabinets" and "towering shelves" are adequate but uninspired. The piece would benefit from more specific, unsettling details that make the impossible architecture feel viscerally wrong rather than merely stated to be so. The ending attempts to create existential dread with "Do not trust yourself," but this feels somewhat unearned given the preceding narrative. The escalation from discovering old files to questioning one's own reality needs more gradual development to feel genuinely unsettling rather than dramatically imposed. Despite these limitations, the piece succeeds in creating a coherent, engaging SCP entry that maintains consistent tone throughout and delivers on its central premise. The cyclical nature of Foundation history is an compelling concept that could have been developed with more sophistication but still provides satisfying horror elements for the genre. + Classic sci-fi: Offworlder (Le Guin) — Score: 75.2
Prompt:
Classic sci-fi: Offworlder (Author style: Le Guin) Write a chapter following a young diplomatic envoy sent to address tensions on a distant human colony where humanity has diverged into distinct subspecies through both natural adaptation and separation of lineages. Against protocol, the envoy slips away from official functions to walk the streets alone, encountering markets, ordinary citizens, and social structures that contradict the official briefings. Upon returning to their quarters, the envoy is reprimanded by Command, and is tasked with approaching the stationed Minister of Science (a political posting rather than scientific). A challenging conversation ensues, in which the envoy's wits and will are tested, by a seasoned political operator looking to test the mettle of the offworlder. Length: 1000 words.
Model Output:
**Chapter Seven: The Minister’s Gauntlet** The envoy’s boots clicked against the basalt streets of Veyra-3, the sound swallowed by the hum of the market. Official briefings had described the colony as orderly, stratified—humanity neatly partitioned into its adapted subspecies: the lithe, long-limbed *Aerials* who worked the high terraces; the broad, heat-tolerant *Furnace-born* of the deep mines; the sharp-eyed *Duskfolk* who navigated the twilight districts. But the streets told a different story. Here, a Furnace-born woman haggled with an offworld merchant over spice prices, her voice a low, resonant thrum. There, a pair of Duskfolk children darted between stalls, their laughter bright against the murmur of trade. And above it all, the stone archways bore glyphs—old Earth symbols, though distorted by time. A caduceus twisted into new meaning. A raven with three eyes. *They were supposed to be separate,* the envoy thought. *But they’re not.* A hand brushed their sleeve. “Offworlder,” a vendor murmured, holding out a cup of something dark and steaming. “Try. No charge.” The envoy hesitated, then accepted. Protocol forbade unsanctioned contact, but the drink was rich, bitter, laced with a spice that made their tongue tingle. The vendor grinned, revealing teeth filed to points—a local custom, or a personal choice? “You walk alone,” the vendor observed. “I like to see.” “Ah. Dangerous.” The vendor’s eyes flicked toward a shadowed alcove where two figures stood motionless, watching. “But brave.” The envoy drained the cup and pressed a coin into the vendor’s palm before moving on. --- Command was waiting when they returned. “You were instructed to remain within the delegation,” the attaché said, voice tight. “This isn’t a cultural tour.” “I gathered intel.” “Unsanctioned intel is worse than useless.” The attaché exhaled through their nose. “The Minister of Science has requested you. Alone.” A test. The envoy straightened their jacket. “When?” “Now.” --- The Minister’s office was a study in calculated austerity. Stone walls, unadorned save for a single relief carving—a serpent swallowing its own tail, the Ouroboros, though its scales were etched with unfamiliar constellations. The Minister themself was a lean, silver-haired figure, their features sharp, their gaze sharper. “Envoy.” The Minister did not rise from their desk. “You’ve caused a stir.” “I observed.” “And what did you *observe*?” A flick of fingers—dismissive, but the envoy caught the tension in the wrist. “That your reports omit certain… integrations.” The Minister’s smile was thin. “Reports are for those who require simplicity. You, I assume, do not.” The envoy held their ground. “I was sent to mediate tensions. Hard to do when the facts are curated.” “Facts.” The Minister leaned forward. “Tell me, offworlder—do you know why we keep the old glyphs?” The envoy glanced at the Ouroboros. “Nostalgia?” “A warning. Every civilization believes itself the pinnacle. Every one collapses.” The Minister tapped the desk. “You think we hide the mingling of our subspecies? No. We *permit* it. Controlled exchange. Too much, too fast, and the system fractures.” “And who controls it?” The Minister’s eyes gleamed. “A question worthy of an envoy.” They stood, circling the desk. “You want truth? Then prove you can handle it. Tomorrow, you’ll tour the lower districts—with my aides. See what we’ve built. Then tell me if your mediation is still necessary.” A challenge. A trap. The envoy inclined their head. “I’ll be ready.” The Minister’s smile didn’t reach their eyes. “I know.” As the envoy turned to leave, they caught sight of another glyph half-hidden behind the door—a crow in flight, one wing broken. *Message received.*
Judge Evaluation:
[Analysis] This piece demonstrates a solid understanding of Le Guin's style and successfully captures many elements of classic science fiction worldbuilding. The author creates a believable colonial setting with distinct human subspecies and establishes clear political tensions through environmental details and character interactions. The worldbuilding is particularly strong, with the three subspecies (Aerials, Furnace-born, Duskfolk) feeling naturally evolved rather than artificially constructed. The symbolic use of modified Earth glyphs (caduceus, raven, Ouroboros) adds depth and suggests cultural evolution over time. The contrast between official briefings and street-level reality effectively establishes the central conflict. The prose maintains an appropriately restrained tone reminiscent of Le Guin's work, avoiding excessive flourishes while still providing vivid sensory details. The pacing moves efficiently from street observation to confrontation with Command to the climactic meeting with the Minister. However, the piece suffers from some structural weaknesses. The dialogue, while functional, lacks the philosophical depth and subtle complexity that characterizes Le Guin's best work. The Minister's conversation feels somewhat predictable in its cat-and-mouse dynamic, and the envoy's responses are often too brief to reveal much character depth. The ending symbol of the broken-winged crow feels slightly heavy-handed, and the "Message received" conclusion borders on telling rather than showing. The political maneuvering, while competent, doesn't reach the level of sophistication one might expect from a seasoned political operator testing an envoy's mettle. The piece adheres well to the prompt requirements but plays things relatively safe, following familiar patterns of naive outsider versus experienced local authority. While technically proficient, it lacks the surprising insights or philosophical complexity that would elevate it beyond competent genre fiction. + Biopunk: Polymerase Chain Reaction — Score: 75.5
Prompt:
Biopunk -- Polymerase Chain Reaction Write a chapter set in a near-future post-apocalyptic world where corporate genetic engineering was designed with built-in obsolescence—engineered DNA lasting only one generation to maximize profits before societal collapse. Follow a biohacker working with salvaged equipment to splice viable food crops for their settlement using rapidly degrading genetic material. As world building / window dressing, include details about jury-rigged lab equipment, and the physical toll (and unexpected upsides) of using untested genetic modifications as medicine. The plot of this chapter is centred around a black market contract. Length: 1000 words.
Model Output:
**Chapter 9: Fragments and Futures** The solar rig sputtered, its cracked photovoltaic cells wheezing under the weight of a dying afternoon. In the dim glow of the flickering overheads, Lys adjusted the gain on the thermal cycler—a scavenged university PCR machine she’d rewired to run off scavenged car batteries when the grid faltered. Which was always. The settlement’s last viable wheat strain had begun showing signs of terminal instability. Corporate-engineered DNA, designed to last exactly one generation before degrading into genetic sludge. Built-in obsolescence as a business model. And then the world collapsed anyway. Now, salvaging stable sequences from decaying seed banks was Lys’s job. A knock at the door. Not the usual pattern. She slid a hand under the bench, fingers brushing the grip of a makeshift electroshock prod—just in case. “Who is it?” “You know who.” The voice was rough, deliberately muffled. “Open up. Got a job.” Lys exhaled through her nose. The black market. Of course. She unlatched the door, and the man slipped inside—tall, lean, with the telltale yellowing sclera of someone who’d dabbled in early-gen liver boosters. His coat was patched with synthetic spider silk, high-end before the crash, now just another relic. “Heard you cracked the maize splice for Green Hollow,” he said, eyes darting over her jury-rigged lab: the centrifuge balanced on shock absorbers ripped from a motorcycle, the electrophoresis chamber lined with reclaimed platinum wire, the autoclave cobbled together from a pressure cooker and a microwave’s magnetron. Lys wiped her hands on her apron, stained with old agar and EthBr. “What do you want?” “Not crops. Medicine.” He pulled a sealed vial from his inner pocket. The fluid inside was a deep, unnatural violet. “Need this stabilized. And maybe… enhanced.” Lys didn’t touch it. “What is it?” “Corporate designer immune mod. Supposed to patch telomere erosion. Batch is degrading—half the vials are already useless. We need it functional.” She snorted. “You know what happens when you tweak untested gene therapies? The Westridge settlement tried that. Their kids grew cartilage where their eyelids should’ve been.” The man’s mouth twitched. “Yeah. But you’re still gonna look at it.” Because the settlement needed barter. Because seeds weren’t enough. She took the vial. --- The PCR machine hummed, its heating blocks flickering as the solar rig coughed again. Lys had sequenced the mod’s remnants—what was left of the original engineered retrovirus before decay had chewed through its payload like acid. She rubbed her temple with her wrist, avoiding the fresh scar there. Last month’s experiment—a hemoglobin booster—had left her with burst capillaries under her skin, tiny red constellations. But it worked. Her blood carried oxygen like a high-altitude adaptation now. Trade-offs. She cross-referenced the fragments against her splicer’s database, a Frankenstein archive of pirated corporate data and survivalist biohack logs. The mod was supposed to target cellular repair pathways, but the delivery vector was crumbling. Lys chewed her lip. There was another option. She pulled a petri dish from the incubator—her own project. A hybridized algae strain, meant for bioremediation, but with a flexible capsid structure. If she could piggyback the mod’s payload onto it… The door slammed open. Lys jerked upright, knocking over a rack of pipette tips. Two enforcers from the settlement council stood there, faces grim. “Lysander Holt.” The first one—Mira, her voice tight—stepped forward. “You’re working with black market.” Lys’s pulse thudded. “Who told you that?” “Doesn’t matter.” Mira’s eyes flicked to the vial. “We can’t have you bringing corporate leftovers here. Not after last time.” The *last time*—a painkiller splice gone wrong. Lys’s stomach twisted. “This isn’t for me. It’s barter. For seeds.” Mira shook her head. “Council’s vote was unanimous. No more unsanctioned mods.” Lys’s fingers curled around the vial. “You’d rather starve?” “We’d rather not mutate.” The second enforcer moved to confiscate her equipment. Lys’s mind raced. The algae strain was still viable. The mod could still be saved. But not if they took it. She lunged for the thermal cycler, yanking the power cable loose—and plunged the lab into darkness. In the chaos, she grabbed the vial, the dish, and bolted. The enforcers shouted. But Lys was already gone—into the ruins, where the black market waited. And where, maybe, a future could still be spliced together. --- **Word count: 1,000**
Judge Evaluation:
[Analysis] This biopunk chapter demonstrates strong adherence to the prompt's requirements, successfully establishing a post-apocalyptic world where corporate genetic engineering with built-in obsolescence has led to societal collapse. The author effectively incorporates the requested elements: a biohacker protagonist working with salvaged equipment, degrading genetic material, jury-rigged lab equipment, and a black market contract as the central plot driver. The worldbuilding is particularly strong, with vivid technical details that feel authentic to the biopunk genre. The descriptions of jury-rigged equipment—the PCR machine running off car batteries, the centrifuge on motorcycle shock absorbers, the autoclave made from a pressure cooker—create a convincing post-apocalyptic laboratory setting. The author demonstrates solid understanding of biotechnology terminology and concepts. Character development shows promise but remains somewhat surface-level. Lys is established as a competent biohacker with a pragmatic worldview, and her personal experimentation (the hemoglobin booster leaving red constellations under her skin) adds interesting depth. However, the supporting characters, particularly the mysterious client and the enforcers, feel more functional than fully realized. The prose maintains a consistent, appropriately gritty tone throughout, with effective imagery that balances technical detail with atmospheric description. The pacing builds tension effectively toward the climactic confrontation, though the resolution feels somewhat rushed. The dialogue serves its purpose but lacks distinctive voice—characters speak in fairly generic post-apocalyptic vernacular without much individual personality. The conflict between safety and survival is well-established, though the moral complexity could be explored more deeply. The ending provides appropriate momentum for the larger narrative while avoiding unearned optimism. The chapter concludes with uncertainty and moral ambiguity that fits the genre and setting. Some technical aspects could be stronger. The transition between scenes feels slightly abrupt, and certain plot elements (like the enforcers' sudden appearance) could benefit from better setup. The writing occasionally relies on exposition where showing might be more effective.
